# The analysand as a musician, type of melody, transitions, text-musical composition...

The analysand as a musician, type of melody, transitions, text-musical composition

When analyzing the figure of the analysand as a musician, we can approach this relationship in terms of musical metaphors that reflect the analytical process and subjective production. Music, as a form of expression, has parallels with analysis in several aspects:

1. **Type of Melody**

- **Elaboration Process** : The melody can be seen as the flow of the analysand's speech, a narrative that unfolds throughout the sessions. Just as a melody evolves through different tones and modulations, the analysand's speech can also change according to free associations and unconscious revelations.

- **Improvisation vs. Structure**: The analysand can be compared to a musician who sometimes improvises his speech and thoughts, but other times follows a predetermined structure, such as the repetitive patterns of his defenses or symptoms.

2. **Transitions** - **Changes of Theme**: In the analytical process, the analysand frequently changes themes, just as in a musical composition there are transitions between different sections or movements. These transitions can reflect passages from one psychic register to another (Imaginary, Symbolic and Real), or changes from one subject to another as free associations arise.

- **Emotional Modulations**: Musical transitions can be compared to the emotional changes in the analysand, who moves between moods and affects as he explores his unconscious contents.

3. **Musical Text Composition** - **Subjective Production**: The act of composing a musical text can be seen as a parallel to the analytical work, where the analysand composes his subjective narrative. The discourse is structured like a score, where the words are notes that express emotions, desires and traumas.

- **Polyphony of Internal Voices**: Just as multiple voices and melodic lines can coexist in a musical composition, in the analysis the analysand can reveal a polyphony of desires, identifications and fantasies. These elements coexist and sometimes come into conflict, as well as musical voices that can create harmony or dissonance.

4. **Rhythm and Repetition**

- **Symptomatic Repetition**: Rhythm can symbolize the repetitive patterns that the analysand brings to the analysis, such as repetitions of behaviors, fantasies or defenses. These repetitions are like a musical motif that returns at different moments in the work.

- **Intervals and Silences**: Silences in music, as in the analysand's speech, are significant. Pauses in the speech can indicate moments of resistance or reflection, and are as important as the words that are spoken. 5. **Creative Act**
- **Sublimation**: The musical production of the analysand can be seen as a form of sublimation, where impulses and affects are transformed into a creative work. The composition process can be a means of working through unconscious issues and transforming them into something aesthetic and shareable.

- **Performance and Listening**: Just as a musician depends on an audience to interpret his work, the analysand needs the analyst as a listener for his narrative to make sense. Analysis is, in itself, a form of "performance" where the analysand reveals and rediscovers himself through listening to the other.

These metaphors can enrich the understanding of the analysand as a musician, showing how the analytical process can be compared to the creative process in music.

# Paternal Function in Lacan

The **Paternal Function** in Lacan is a crucial concept for understanding the constitution of the subject in the field of psychoanalysis. It is directly related to the structuring of desire, the law, and the subject's entry into the symbolic world. Below are the main points about **FPaternal anointing** :1. **Name-of-the-Father** - The **Name-of-the-Father** is a central term in Lacan's theory, which symbolizes the father's function as an authority that institutes the **Law**. It does not refer only to the biological father, but to the symbolic function that any figure or element can exercise, representing a fundamental prohibition.

- The prohibition that the Name-of-the-Father represents is the prohibition of incest, which prevents the subject from fulfilling his desire for the mother. By blocking this desire, the father introduces the child into the field of desire and language, since he needs to seek satisfaction for his desire outside the mother, in the social and symbolic field.

2. **Paternal Metaphor** - Lacan formulates the **paternal metaphor** to explain how the Name-of-the-Father overlaps the mother's desire, replacing it and structuring the subject's desire. The equation of the paternal metaphor is:
   $$
   \text{Mother's Desire (Dm)} + \text{Father's Name (Np)} = Subject
   $$

Here, the Father's Name intervenes to block the mother's desire, allowing the subject to develop his own desiring position. 3. **Paternal Function as Prohibition**

- The **Paternal Function** institutes the **Law** in the unconscious, functioning as a limit to enjoyment (jouissance) and unbridled desire. By imposing this law, the symbolic father allows the subject to separate from the mother and enter the world of language, where social and symbolic exchanges are possible.

- This prohibition should not be seen as a punishment, but as a necessary structure for the development of the subject. It allows desire to organize itself around the lack and the search for the **Other**.

4. **Foraclusion of the Name-of-the-Father**

- The **foraclusion** (or rejection) of the Name-of-the-Father is at the root of psychosis, according to Lacan. When the Name-of-the-Father is rejected from the symbolic field, the subject is unable to integrate the Law and desire becomes unregulated. In cases of psychosis, the absence of this function leads to a destructuring of the symbolic world, resulting in hallucinations, delusions and confusion in language.

5. **Sublimation and the Paternal Function**

- The Paternal Function is also important in regulating impulses and in the possibility of **sublimation**. Desire, when structured by the Law, can be diverted to socially accepted objectives, such as artistic, intellectual or productive work, instead of manifesting itself as destructive or forbidden enjoyment.

6. **Paternal Function and the Desiring Subject**

- The **Paternal Function** contributes to the formation of the subject as a desiring subject. By introducing the subject to language and law, the Name-of-the-Father creates the lack, which is essential for the emergence of desire. The subject finds himself blocked in his search for immediate enjoyment and is led to relate to symbolic objects that replace the figure of the mother.

7. **Symbolic, Imaginary and Real Dimension**

- Lacan also distinguishes the Paternal Function in terms of its effects in the **Symbolic**, **Imaginary** and **Real** registers:
- **Symbolic**: The father is the figure who introduces the law and regulates desire.
- **Imaginary**: The father can be idealized or seen as a rival, creating dynamics of identification and competition.
- **Real**: The father can represent an absolute limit to enjoyment, something that can never be completely integrated into the symbolic and that remains as a point of trauma or excess in the subject.
  In short, the **Paternal Function** in Lacan is fundamental for the development of the subject in the symbolic field and in the structuring of desire. It operates as an interdiction that allows the subject to position himself in relation to the Other and to the laws that govern desire and enjoyment.

# Maternal function in Lacan

The **Maternal Function** in Lacan is related to the role of the mother in the subjective constitution of the subject, especially in her role of providing care, presence and introduction to the field of desire. Unlike the **Paternal Function**, which is related to interdiction and law, the Maternal Function is more linked to desire, the satisfaction of needs and the emotional involvement of the subject. Here are the main points about the **Maternal Function** in theoryLacanian theory: 1. **The Mother as the First Other**

- In the baby's life, the mother (or the one who occupies the role of mother) is the first Other with whom the subject relates. She is the source of care, nourishment and satisfaction of the most immediate needs. At first, the baby does not perceive the mother as a being separate from itself, but as an extension of its own existence.

- The mother, therefore, represents the subject's first access to the world and to symbolic exchanges, since it is through the mother's gaze and words that the baby begins to construct its first experiences of language and meaning.

2. **The Mother's Desire**

- The **mother's desire** is a central force in the development of the subject, since the child is constantly seeking to understand and satisfy what the mother desires. The subject is formed by trying to capture the mother's desire and, at the same time, being marked by uncertainty about what this desire really is.

- The mother's desire is not only the attention she gives to the baby, but something more complex that involves her own desires, which transcend the child. This creates a gap in the baby, who realizes that he is not the only object of the mother's desire, which is fundamental for the development of the subject.

3. **The Mother as Satisfaction and Lack**

- The Maternal Function involves the balance between providing the baby with the satisfaction of his needs and creating a space of lack. The mother cannot always be available, and this absence introduces the subject to the idea that the Other is not completely at his disposal.

- By allowing the baby to feel lack and absence, the mother contributes to the subject's entry into the field of desire. Lack is necessary for desire to arise, and it is the mother who, by regulating her presence and absence, introduces the subject to the dynamics of desire. 4. **Symbolic, Imaginary and Real**
- In the **Imaginary** register, the relationship with the mother is a mirroring relationship, where the baby identifies with the mother and seeks to be the object of her love and desire.

- In the **Symbolic** register, the mother is the one who introduces the subject to the language and the field of the Other, by naming the baby and initiating the first symbolic exchanges. She is also the first to point out the presence of a lack, which will be filled by the Name-of-the-Father, in the process of symbolic separation between mother and child.

- In the **Real** register, the mother can represent excess or "enjoyment" (jouissance), an involvement that goes beyond the symbolic dimension and can be suffocating or excessive, without limits. The paternal function, by intervening, creates a point of separation between the child and the mother, regulating this relationship. 5. **The Role of the Maternal Function in Separation**
- The Maternal Function prepares the subject for separation, which will be operated by the **Paternal Function**. The mother, by limiting her presence and not completely merging with the child, allows the subject to develop his own position in the field of desire and language.

- The intervention of the **Name-of-the-Father** (the prohibition of incestuous desire by the mother) is what allows the subject to separate from the mother and inscribe himself in the symbolic field. However, it is the Maternal Function that initiates this separation by introducing lack and desire into the subject.

6. **Sufficiently Symbolizable Mother**

- Lacan speaks of the idea of ​​a "sufficiently symbolizable mother" or "sufficiently symbolizable Other". This means that, for the healthy development of the subject, the mother must be able to occupy a symbolic position and not just an imaginary or real one. She must be open to the dimension of lack, accepting that her desire cannot be completely realized through the child.

- A mother who does not allow this symbolization can imprison the subject in a relationship of excessive enjoyment or fusion, without introducing the lack necessary for the development of desire.

7. **Maternal Function and the Social Bond**

- The Maternal Function is also related to the insertion of the subject in the social bond, since the mother is the first to transmit language and culture to the baby. She mediates the subject's entry into the symbolic and cultural world by naming it, speakingwith him and place him in relation to the Other (the father, society, language).

8. **Excess of Maternal Function**

- When the Maternal Function becomes excessive, there may be a form of invasion or overload of the subject, resulting in a type of imprisonment in maternal desire. This may manifest itself in pathologies such as psychosis, where symbolic separation does not occur adequately, and the subject may become trapped in this relationship of excessive enjoyment with the mother.
  In short, the **Maternal Function** in Lacan involves the role of the mother in the constitution of the subject as a desiring person, introducing him to language, desire and lack. She is responsible for a primordial relationship of care and satisfaction, but also for the introduction of lack and preparation for the separation that will be operated by the Paternal Function.

# Identification of the son or daughter with the father or mother...

Identification of the son or daughter with the father or mother

The **identification of the son or daughter with the father or mother** is a fundamental process in the formation of subjectivity and in the construction of identity in the field of psychoanalysis, especially in Lacan's theory. Identifications with parental figures help to shape the subject in relation to desire, law and the symbolic field.1. **Primary and Secondary Identification**

- **Primary identification** occurs early and unconsciously, being the first form of construction of subjectivity based on the relationship with parents or caregivers. This identification is not based on a conscious choice, but on a deep internalization of parental figures or their traits.

- **Secondary identification** occurs at a later stage and is linked to more complex processes, such as the internalization of parental traits or characteristics in moments of conflict, as in the **Oedipus complex**. The child identifies with both the father and the mother in different ways, as his or her desiring position develops.

2. **Identification with the Father**

- **Identification with the father** is related to the paternal function, which is fundamental for the subject's introduction to the symbolic law and the prohibition of incest. The child, by identifying with the father, internalizes the law and the role of mediator between the mother's desire and the social field.

- In the Oedipus complex, the child may see the father as a rival for the mother's desire, but also as a figure to be identified with. The father thus represents both the authority (which prohibits incestuous desire) and the model of the subject that relates to desire in the symbolic field.

- For boys, this identification with the father may be marked by the desire to take his place in the future, adopting his position in desire and in the law. For girls, this identification may manifest itself in different ways, with the internalization of the father's symbolic function.

3. **Identification with the Mother**

- **Identification with the mother** is strongly linked to the subject's first relationship with desire and the maternal function. The mother, as the first Other with whom the subject relates, is a central figure of identification, especially at the beginning of life.

- For girls, identification with the mother can represent a gender continuity, where the daughter identifies with the feminine role that the mother occupies in desire and in relationships with the Other. For boys, identification with the mother can also occur, but it is often associated with the desire to "take the father's place" in the relationship with the mother.

- However, identification with the mother is also marked by the perception that she is not enough on her own, that is, the mother also has a desire beyond her child. This recognition leads to the perception that the subject is not everything to the mother, which is fundamental for the constitution of subjectivity and for the emergence of desire. 4. **Oedipus Complex and Identification**
- The **Oedipus complex** is central to Lacan in terms of the identification process. During this phase, the son or daughter finds themselves in a triangular relationship with the father and mother.The child desires the exclusive love of the mother, but perceives the presence of the father as a figure that prevents the fulfillment of this desire.

- Identification with the father or mother during the Oedipus complex is ambivalent: on the one hand, the child sees the parents as rivals, but on the other hand, he also takes them as models. The resolution of the Oedipus complex implies an identification with the father (or with the paternal function) as a representative of the law and symbolic order.

- From the identification with the father and the mother, the subject finds his place in the field of desire, learning to deal with lack and the law.

5. **Gender Difference in Identification**

- Lacan emphasizes that the identification process is different for boys and girls, depending on the way they deal with **sexualization** and **symbolic castration**.

- For boys, identification with the father is seen as the path to accepting symbolic castration and entering the field of desire, regulated by law. The boy learns to deal with desire and lack from the prohibition represented by the father.

- For girls, identification with the mother may involve a more complex negotiation with desire, since feminine desire is often represented as an enigma in Lacanian theory. The girl identifies with the mother, but also seeks to understand her desire, which is marked by absence and by the Other.

6. **Identification and Symbolic Function**

- Both identification with the father and with the mother have a symbolic function in the constitution of the subject. It is not a mere imitation, but an internalization of the functions that each one exercises in the field of desire and law.

- The **Paternal Function** represents the law and prohibition, regulating enjoyment and desire. The **Maternal Function**, in turn, is the one that introduces the subject to desire and the field of the Other. Identification with these functions, therefore, is fundamental for the insertion of the subject in the social bond and in language. 7. **Identification and Symptom** - Identifications can also manifest themselves in the form of **symptoms**. When there are conflicts or impasses in identification with the parents, the subject may develop symptoms that express these difficulties. For example, a very strong or fusional identification with the mother may lead to difficulty in separating from her and finding one's own space in desire.

- Psychosis, for example, can be seen as the result of a failure in the **foraclusion** of the Name-of-the-Father, where the subject is unable to identify adequately with the paternal function and integrate the symbolic law.

8. **Identification and Desire** - Ultimately, identifications with the father or mother are fundamental to the constitution of the desiring subject. The subject constitutes himself as a being of desire by situating himself in relation to the desire of the parents and the place they occupy in the symbolic field.

- Identification with the father and mother allows the subject to construct his or her own position in relation to desire and lack, as well as his or her place in the social bond and in language.

In short, identification with the father or mother in Lacan is closely linked to the process of constitution of the subject, to the entry into the field of desire and law, and to the way in which the subject deals with lack, desire and jouissance. It is through these identifications that the subject finds his or her place in the symbolic and social field.

# Father as provider of penis for mother, money, loyalty to family, professional inheritance, Social circulation for children...

Father as provider of penis for mother, money, loyalty to family, professional inheritance, Social circulation for childrenThis content may violate our [usage policies](https://openai.com/policies/usage-policies) .

In Lacan's psychoanalytic theory, the **father** plays a fundamental role in family dynamics and in the subjective constitution of the subject, being seen not only as an authority figure, but also as the one who brings a series of symbolic and social elements that regulate desire and the family bond. The view of the father as **provider** of certain elementsfor the mother and the family involves several dimensions, which can be understood both at the psychic and social levels.1. **Pai como Provedor de Pênis para a Mãe**

- In the context of the **Oedipus complex**, Lacan reformulates the Freudian view and presents the father as the one who **holds the phallus** (symbolic). The phallus is not the genital organ itself, but a metaphor for power and desire. The father is the one who symbolically "owns" the phallus and is able to grant or deny it to the mother.

- The son or daughter realizes that the father offers the mother something that he (the child) cannot offer: the symbolic phallus. This recognition is central to **symbolic castration**, where the child understands that he is not the center of maternal desire, since the father occupies this position of provider of desire and lack.

- The phallus, in this sense, is a **signifier** that regulates desire in the relationship between the father, the mother and the child, and to which the subject submits in order to enter the field of law and language.

2. **Pai como Provedor de Dinheiro**

- The father's role as **material provider** also comes into play in psychoanalysis. The father is the one who, traditionally, occupies the position of financial support for the family, providing resources that guarantee the family's subsistence and well-being.

- In symbolic terms, money can be seen as an extension of the phallus. Just like the phallus, money represents an element of power and circulation, which organizes family and social relationships.

- The figure of the father as economic provider is also a manifestation of the **circulation of goods** and symbolic values, which regulate social and family relations.

3. **Fidelidade à Família**

- The father, in his symbolic role, is also seen as the one who guarantees **family coherence and stability**, being responsible for **fidelity** to the family. This includes the role of maintaining the family unit, protecting marital bonds, and ensuring that children follow family norms and traditions.

- Fidelity, both to one's wife and children, is not just a question of behavior, but of transmitting values, respecting social and moral laws, and **preserving family honor**.

- The father, in this context, embodies the **symbolic law**, which regulates desire and sexuality within the family sphere. By being faithful to the family, the father acts as a guarantor of the norms that structure the social bond.

4. **Herança Profissional**

- Professional inheritance is another important dimension of the paternal role. In many cultures, the father plays the role of **transmitter of a professional tradition** or of a legacy that passes from generation to generation.

- In psychoanalysis, this transmission can be seen as part of the **desire of the Other**: the father transmits to the son not only material goods or professional knowledge, but also **expectations, desires and norms** that regulate the insertion of the subject in the social and professional world.

- This inheritance can be both **symbolic** (in the form of values ​​and expectations) and **concrete** (as in the case of professions or family businesses that pass from parents to children).

5. **Circulação Social para os Filhos**

- The father also has a crucial role in the **social insertion of his children**. In many cases, he is the mediator who introduces children to the social sphere, connecting them to networks of relationships that guarantee their insertion into the world.

- This function involves the transmission of **cultural values, social norms and kinship ties**, allowing the child to locate himself in the **symbolic field** and find his place in society. The social circulation that the father provides is, therefore, part of the subject's inscription in the world of others, beyond the family nucleus.

- The father, as a representative of the law and norm, ensures that his children come into contact with the **social Other**, connecting them to the dynamics of social and economic exchanges, as well as introducing them to spheres of power and influence. social.

6. **Pai e Função Simbólica**

- Para Lacan, o pai não é apenas uma fural figure, but, above all, a symbolic function that guarantees the **organization of the family and social structure**. The father represents the **law of desire** that regulates the relationships between the subject, the mother and the broader social field.

- The **Paternal Function** involves the prohibition of incest, the introduction of the subject into language and the symbolic field, and the mediation between the subject and the Other. It is this function that allows the subject to separate from the mother and enter the field of desire, under the regulation of the law.

- The **transmission of values ​​and inheritances** by the father — whether material, professional or symbolic — is a way of ensuring that the subject is inserted into the social field and the symbolic order, regulating his place in desire and in society.

### Conclusion

The father's function as **provider** involves multiple aspects: from the symbolic prohibition that regulates the desire of the mother and the child, to material support, fidelity to the family and the transmission of cultural and professional inheritances. The father figure plays the role of mediator between the family and society, ensuring the inclusion of children in the symbolic and social field.

# Mother as recipient of the father's penis, money, loyalty to the family, childcare and home, friendship with other womenThis content may violate our [usage policies](https://openai.com/policies/usage-policies) .

From a Lacanian psychoanalytic perspective, the figure of the **mother** assumes a central role in desire, family relationships and in the process of subjectivation of children. Just as the father has his symbolic functions, the mother occupies a specific place related to care, desire and the symbolic function. The mother's role goes beyond the biological, being mediated by relations of desire, social laws and cultural expectations. The mother is therefore seen as the recipient of various elements coming from the father and society, which place her in a complex position within the family structure.1. **Mother as Recipient of the Father's Penis**

- In Lacan's theory, the mother is described as the one who **desires the (symbolic) phallus, and the father is the one who holds it. This dynamic is central to the **Oedipus complex**, where the child perceives that the mother desires something that she herself cannot directly provide — the **phallus\*\*.

- The penis, in this case, is not understood literally, but rather as a **signifier of power, lack and desire**. The mother desires the phallus, and the father is the one who symbolically possesses and offers it.

- The child's recognition that the father "gives" the phallus to the mother introduces the idea that the mother is not self-sufficient and that her desire goes beyond the mother-child relationship. This helps the child to situate himself in the field of desire and symbolic castration. 2. **Mother as Recipient of Money** - Traditionally, the father was seen as the **financial provider** of the family, and the mother as the **receiver** of these resources, responsible for managing the home and ensuring the well-being of the children. This dynamic reflects historically established gender roles.

- In psychoanalysis, money can be seen as a **signifier** of the paternal function, an extension of the phallus that organizes the social and material life of the family. The mother, upon receiving this financial support, uses it to ensure the organization of domestic and family life.

- However, this position also places the mother in a relationship of symbolic dependence on the father, since she is in the position of managing what is provided to her.

3. **Fidelity to the Family** - The mother has an important role in **maintaining family cohesion** and creating a stable environment for her children. Her **fidelity to the family** is manifested in her commitment to her children, in caring for her husband, and in organizing domestic life.

- For psychoanalysis, the mother also embodies the **symbolic law** within the family space, since she is the one who initially regulates the child's first relationships of love, care and desire.The mother's fidelity to the family can be seen as an expression of the desire to maintain the integrity of family ties.

- Furthermore, this fidelity is not just a question of behavior, but is closely linked to the **transmission of norms and values** that shape the subjectivity of children, helping them to insert themselves into the symbolic order.

4. **Mother and Care for Children and the Home**

- The mother traditionally plays the role of **primary caregiver**, responsible for the education and well-being of her children. This care involves both physical and emotional needs, creating the environment in which the subject begins to constitute himself.

- For Lacan, the mother is the first **Other** with whom the subject relates, and this relationship is marked by **desire and demand**. The mother takes care of her children, but at the same time she desires something that goes beyond them. This maternal desire is what introduces the child to the symbolic field, leading him to recognize that he is not everything to the mother.

- The mother's care for the home and children can also be seen as part of her **symbolic function**, as she ensures the stability of the environment in which the subject develops, while also setting limits and introducing the first rules of coexistence.

5. **Mother and Friendship with Other Women**

- The mother's **relationship with other women** also has psychoanalytic implications. In social terms, these friendships can represent a form of support and solidarity, but on a symbolic level, they can be seen as spaces where the mother shares her experiences of desire, motherhood and femininity.

- In psychoanalysis, the relationship between women can be understood as a space for exchanging **experiences of desire and care**, which often escape the male gaze. Friendship between women can represent a way of sharing experiences that are, to a certain extent, marginalized by the paternal function and the patriarchal symbolic structure.

- Furthermore, these friendships can function as a form of emotional support for the mother, allowing her to relate to other women who are in the same position as caregivers and responsible for the family, creating a network of affection and understanding.

6. **Mother and Symbolic Function**

- The mother, for Lacan, is not only the physical caregiver of her children, but also plays an important symbolic function. She is the first primordial other, the one who introduces the subject to the field of desire and language.

- The mother desires the phallus, but she is also the one who gives the name to the child's desire, by caring for it and situating it in relation to the lack. Through the relationship with the mother, the child begins to perceive that there is something beyond her, a maternal desire that the child cannot completely fulfill.

- This perception is fundamental to the constitution of the subject, as it is what leads the child to identify with the lack and to enter the field of the symbolic, where he can position himself in relation to the desire of the Other. 7. **Mother and Social Circulation**
- The mother, although traditionally seen as the central figure in the domestic space, also plays a role in the **social circulation of her children**. She introduces her children to their first **social relationships**, teaches them norms of coexistence and creates bonds with other families and social networks.

- In addition, she may be responsible for transmitting certain **cultural and social values**, helping her children to situate themselves within the symbolic and social field in which they are inserted. The mother not only takes care of her children, but also **mediates their relationship with the world** around them.

### Conclusion

The mother, in Lacanian psychoanalysis, is much more than a simple caregiver. She is a central figure in the **field of desire**, being the recipient of the symbolic phallus, money and the protection offered by the father. At the same time, she plays a vital role in maintaining the family, caring for her children and transmitting the values ​​and norms that structure subjectivity. Her symbolic function, in addition to thematerial and emotional aspect, ensures that children can enter the symbolic and social field, while at the same time she relates to other women and social networks that support her role as mother and wife.

# Dysphoria between gender and modality of enjoyment, gender and functions that one likes to perform...

Dysphoria between gender and modality of enjoyment, gender and functions that one likes to perform

The relationship between **gender dysphoria** and the modalities of **enjoyment** is a complex and multifaceted theme, especially within the Lacanian psychoanalytic perspective. Lacan explores how enjoyment (or "jouissance") is not merely something linked to sexual pleasure, but is deeply intertwined with symbolic identifications and processes of subjectivation — including issues of **gender**. In this context, gender can be related both to the way in which the subject identifies (masculine, feminine, non-binary, etc.) and to the **functions** and roles that the subject desires or feels satisfaction in performing.1. **Gender Dysphoria and Modality of Enjoyment**

- **Gender dysphoria** refers to the incongruence between the **gender felt or experienced** by a person and the **gender attributed** to them at birth. In psychoanalysis, gender is not simply a biological issue, but a construction deeply linked to the **symbolic**, the **imaginary** and the **real**.

- Lacan speaks of **enjoyment** as something that goes beyond the pleasure governed by the pleasure principle. He distinguishes, for example, **phallic enjoyment** — which is related to the limit and function of the symbolic phallus — from the **enjoyment of the Other**, which is linked to something beyond the symbolic order and is often difficult to access or define.

- Gender dysphoria can be understood, in this context, as a form of **discomfort in relation to one's own symbolic and imaginary position** in the field of enjoyment. The subject who experiences dysphoria feels that the gender assigned to him or her does not correspond to the way in which his or her desire and jouissance are organized. Discomfort with the body, social roles, and gender expectations can be seen as an attempt to reorganize one's **jouissance** in a way that is more congruent with the subject's desire.

2. **Gender and Phallic Identification** - In the Lacanian model, the **phallus** is a central signifier that structures desire and jouissance. Both men and women, regardless of their biological sex, organize themselves around the **symbolic phallus**. Phallic jouissance, in this sense, is not something exclusively masculine, but a modality of jouissance that regulates the **relationship with desire** and with lack.

- **Gender dysphoria** can be seen as a rupture or friction with the way in which the subject organizes himself or herself around the **symbolic phallus**. If the subject does not identify with the gender that has been assigned to him or her, there is a sense of displacement in relation to this symbolic inscription, which affects the way he or she experiences jouissance.

- In addition, Lacan distinguishes between the **masculine position** and the **feminine position** with regard to jouissance, but without rigidly linking them to biological sex. The masculine position is more related to **phallic jouissance**, while the feminine position can open space for the **jouissance of the Other** — a jouissance beyond the phallus, often more difficult to capture or name.

3. **Gender and Functions That One Likes to Perform**

- The **relationship between gender and the functions** or roles that the subject likes to perform is also relevant. What one likes to do, or the activities and functions that bring satisfaction, are closely linked to **desire**, **identification** and **jouissance**.

- Some people may identify with **roles traditionally associated with a certain gender**, such as care (usually associated with the maternal role) or authority (more associated with the paternal role). However, these roles are not intrinsically linked to biological sex, but to the **symbolic field** in which the subject is inscribed.- The pleasure in performing certain functions or roles can be seen as a way of organizing **enjoyment** according to one's own gender identification. Someone who identifies with a gender different from the one assigned to them may find **satisfaction** in performing functions associated with that desired gender, as a way of aligning enjoyment with their own desire and identity.

4. **Maternal and Paternal Function**

- The **maternal function** and the **paternal function**, although traditionally associated with biological sex, are also symbolic functions that can be assumed by any subject, regardless of their gender. Lacan emphasizes that the maternal function is linked to the **desire of the mother** and the relationship with the child, while the paternal function is associated with the **introduction of the symbolic law**, which regulates desire and the prohibition of incest.

- Gender dysphoria can affect the way in which the subject positions himself in relation to these functions. A trans subject, for example, may find satisfaction or discomfort in performing a function that is not culturally associated with the gender with which they identify, creating a **field of tension** between desire, enjoyment and social norms.

5. **Enjoyment and Non-Traditional Functions** - Lacan also introduces the idea that **enjoyment is not completely governed** by the phallus or traditional gender norms. There are **modalities of enjoyment** that escape the simple categorization of masculine/feminine or heteronormative. This is particularly relevant in contexts in which trans or non-binary subjects find their own ways of organizing their enjoyment and their social functions.

- **Gender dysphoria** can be understood as a movement towards a form of **enjoyment that is more congruent with the subject's desire**, breaking with the normative expectations of gender and social function. The subject seeks a way of organizing their enjoyment and desire that is more aligned with their gender identity and the functions they wish to perform. 6. **Enjoyment, Gender and Social Bond**
- The **modality of enjoyment** also influences the way the subject relates to the **social bond** and to others. Gender and enjoyment are intrinsically linked to the ways in which the subject inserts him/herself into the social fabric, in his/her **interpersonal relationships** and in his/her choices of identification.

- Someone who experiences **gender dysphoria** may feel out of place not only in relation to his/her body, but also in relation to social expectations, which affects the way enjoyment manifests itself in relationships with others. The search for roles that bring **satisfaction** may be a way of rediscovering a position of enjoyment that is more integrated and less conflicting with one's own desire.

### Conclusion

The relationship between **gender dysphoria** and **modality of enjoyment** reveals the complexity of gender identifications and the roles that the subject wishes to play. In the Lacanian model, enjoyment is not limited to sexual pleasure, but is deeply related to the symbolic organization of desire. The subject may experience dysphoria when the assigned gender does not correspond to the way in which their enjoyment is organized, and the relief of this dysphoria often involves the search for functions or social roles that are in line with their gender identity and their particular form of enjoyment.

# The unconscious is a tireless worker, any video, text, audio can go viral and technology companies pay for it if it is popular enough

The idea that the **unconscious is a tireless worker** is rooted in Freudian and Lacanian psychoanalysis. The unconscious never stops operating, producing dreams, fantasies, lapses and, above all, manifestations in the subject's language and behavior. This constant activity can be compared to the way technology platforms operate: they are always analyzing and monitoring data in real timereal, looking for patterns and trends. Today, with the growth of social networks and digital content platforms, any production — be it **video, text or audio** — can quickly gain traction and "go viral". This process often does not follow predictable rules and, in a way, can be understood as something that emerges from the "collective unconscious" of society, responding to desires, fantasies and demands that are, to a large extent, unconscious. **Technology companies**, such as Google, YouTube, TikTok and others, profit from this dynamic, promoting popular content and paying content creators as their videos, texts or audios reach a certain level of popularity. The logic behind this is that the more a content resonates with users (that is, the more it captures something of their unconscious desires or fantasies), the more it is shared, viewed and commented on. This virality generates **engagement**, which translates into revenue for these platforms, whether through advertisements, partnerships or direct monetization of content. Thus, the individual unconscious that manifests itself in the production of content can, unintentionally, align itself with the trends of the collective unconscious, generating a viral effect that is exploited by **technology companies**.

# The Other of the Internet, public on the internet

Speaking to the analyst x Speaking to the Internet

The comparison between **speaking to the analyst** and **speaking to the internet** involves a reflection on the difference between two types of "Others": the **Other of psychoanalytic discourse** and the **Other of the Internet**. 1. **The Other in Psychoanalysis**: In psychoanalysis, the **Other** (with a capital letter) represents the symbolic structure that organizes the field of desire, laws, language and the unconscious. When the subject speaks to the analyst, he is not just speaking to a person, but to this **symbolic Other**, which is part of the very structure of the unconscious. The analyst occupies a specific position, one that allows the subject to confront his own desire, lapses and symptoms, without judgment, and allows the emergence of something from his unconscious. Speaking to the analyst is an **intimate exercise**, in which the subject deals with his unconscious formations, believing that the analyst is capable of deciphering something deeper in his discourse. This dialogue is structured around a **void** — a space where desire and lack meet, where something unsaid and enigmatic can emerge. 2. **The Other of the Internet**: In contrast, the **Other of the Internet** is a **collective and diffuse Other**, composed of thousands or millions of spectators, users and algorithms that are ready to judge, react, share or ignore the content produced by the subject. The subject does not speak to a specific listener, but to an undefined audience, often constructing his speech to be as attractive, popular or "viral" as possible. Here, the dialogue is not with the unconscious, but with the expectation of public recognition, social validation and popularity. Speaking to the internet implies dealing with the gaze (real or imagined) of a mass of people who can react immediately. In this context, the **image of the self** (idealized, constructed) becomes central, since interactions on the internet tend to favor the performance and management of one's **persona**. The subject, when speaking to the internet, often seeks to confirm his place in the **social** or **cultural** discourse, more than to reveal his unconscious formations. 3. **Fundamental Differences**:

- **Intimacy vs. Public Exposure**:
  When speaking to the analyst, the subject has an intimate and protected space, where the focus is on revealing something of his own unconscious truth. On the internet, speech is **exposed publicly**, and what is said is subject to quick and often superficial judgments, approvals and disapprovals.

- **Interpretation x Reaction**:
  The analyst interprets the subject's speech, listening beyond the wordswords, in search of lapses, repetitions and unconscious formations. The internet public, on the other hand, reacts directly, immediately and generally superficially, rarely offering a deep interpretation.

- **Desire x Ego**:
  In the analytical space, the subject deals with his desire, confronting his faults and limitations. On the internet, what is often at stake is the construction and maintenance of an image, or **ego-ideal**, fed by social interactions and public approval.

- **Structuring Other x Fragmented Other**:
  The analyst's **Other** is structuring, as it helps the subject to reorganize his discourse and his relationship with desire and language. The **Internet Other**, on the other hand, is fragmented, diffuse and does not offer a solid or coherent structure, but a set of opinions, views and volatile reactions. 4. **Conclusion**: Speaking to the analyst is an experience of confrontation with one's own **unconscious** and the **lack** that structures desire. Speaking to the internet, on the contrary, is a process of exposure to the **gaze of the collective Other**, in which the subject tends to seek external recognition and validation. Both contexts involve a relationship with the Other, but the analyst offers a space for **introspection and symbolic elaboration**, while the internet provides an environment of **immediacy, performance and social reaction**.

# Could the analyst also be a singer/poet?

The idea that the **analyst would also be a singer or poet** can be understood from the nature of **analytic listening** and the use of **language** in psychoanalytic practice.1. **The Analyst as Poet**: The analyst’s listening involves careful attention to what is said and what is not said — the **between the lines**, the **lapses**, the **repetitions** and the **silences** of the analysand’s discourse. This work with language often resembles that of a poet, who also deals with the unsaid, with implicit meanings and with the multiple resonances of words.

- **Economy of language**: Like the poet, the analyst works with the **precision** of words and their multiple layers of meaning. A poet condenses something profound into a few words, and the analyst, by intervening with specific words, seeks to hit the right point in the analysand’s discourse.

- **Symbolic resonance**: Poetry seeks to capture something that escapes the ordinary logic of language, just as the analyst seeks to capture something from the **unconscious**, which is structured like language but escapes the usual grammar. 2. **The Analyst as Singer**: On the other hand, the idea of ​​the analyst as **singer** can also be thought of in terms of the **musicality** of language. Lacan speaks of the importance of **tone**, **rhythm** and **intonation** in discourse, which can reveal as much as the explicit content of the words.
- **Rhythm of the session**: The analyst regulates the **rhythm** of the session\*\*, just as a singer adapts the tempo of the melody. Sometimes, a brief and punctual intervention (a break in the session or a phrase) can be more effective than a long development, just as a note or verse can transform a melody.

- **Melody of speech**: The analysand's discourse also has its own melody, which the analyst tries to follow. The singer deals with the expressiveness of the voice, while the analyst follows the expressiveness in the **use of language**, in its **lapses** and **hesitations**, capturing the emotional and unconscious variations of the subject. 3. **The Analyst and the Reality of Language**: The analyst's practice involves a special relationship with language that can be compared to the art of a poet or singer. However, the analyst goes beyond the aesthetic dimension, as he seeks to **cross the symbolic** to reach something of the **real**, that is, that point where language fails, where words cannot fully account for desire and subjectivity. In this sense, the analyst's function is also to lead the analysand to confront this structural lack oflanguage.4. **Conclusion**: The analyst can be compared to a **poet** or **singer** insofar as he or she deals with **language in a sensitive and careful way**, attentive to nuances and hidden meanings. However, the analyst's goal is not beauty or artistic expression, but to facilitate the **symbolic elaboration** and **subjective transformation** of the analysand. The analyst, like the poet, reveals what is behind the words, but does so with the purpose of opening a path to the subject's unconscious desire and truth.

# Psychotics, borderlines, schizophrenics who are successful on social networks...

Psychotics, borderlines, schizophrenics who are successful on social networks

The presence of **psychotics, borderlines and schizophrenics** who are successful on social networks is a phenomenon that can be analyzed from several perspectives, including psychoanalysis, social psychology and the impact of networks on subjectivity. There are aspects of **identification**, **performativity** and the way in which networks amplify certain traits, generating popularity around personalities that deviate from the traditional social norm.1. **Psychotics and Schizophrenics**: For individuals with **psychotic disorders**, such as schizophrenia, social networks can represent a paradoxical space. On the one hand, they can share their **delusions** or **fragmented views of reality**, something that on the networks can acquire a curious or even fascinating character for the public.

- **Expression without a filter**: Often, the lack of a filter between thought and speech can lead to the expression of surprising, bizarre or intriguing content, which ends up attracting followers. These individuals often present a **fragmented** speech or one loaded with private meanings, which can resonate in an unusual way for the social network audience.

- **Fascination with the unusual**: Social media tends to amplify what is **different** or **out of the ordinary**. Thus, individuals with schizophrenia or other psychotic disorders can attract attention precisely because of their **difference** and their **unconventional** way of interacting with the world.

2. **Borderlines**: Individuals with **borderline personality disorder** have characteristics that can be highly performative on social media. They tend to experience intense emotions and have volatile social relationships, which can generate **drama**, **conflict** and even **intense personal accounts**.

- **Dramaticity and emotional involvement**: The emotional intensity characteristic of borderlines can be seen as something engaging for social media viewers, who often feel attracted to **intense accounts**, whether of pain, love or anger. These authentic and extreme expressions can gain many followers, who identify with the intensity of emotions.

- **Self-image and search for validation**: Individuals with borderline disorder often have an unstable **self-image** and may use social media as a way to seek constant **validation**. This search for recognition can translate into frequent and engaging posts that generate a lot of interaction, likes and comments.

3. **Social Media as a Mirror and a Stage**: Social media functions as a **mirror** and, at the same time, as a **stage** for everyone. In this environment, individuals with disorders such as psychosis or borderline can find a space for expression that allows them to present themselves to the Other (the public) in a spontaneous or calculated way. Success can come from **identification** with what is experienced as a **personal truth**, no matter how delusional or outside the norm it may seem.

- **Curiosity and spectacle**: Social media audiences often consume content with a mixture of curiosity and fascination. The **fragmented speech** or **extreme emotional manifestations** of individuals with these disorders can be seen as a spectacle. There is, fortherefore, an **exploration** of certain characteristics, where the public participates as a voyeur in a process that, in many cases, can be quite painful or chaotic for the person themselves.

- **Validation and virality**: On networks, the logic of **virality** tends to privilege that which causes a **reaction**. Posts that cause astonishment, identification or a mixture of both tend to be shared and commented on more. Individuals with disorders such as schizophrenia or borderline, who frequently express emotional **intensity** or **outside-the-norm** thoughts, can become **viral**, not necessarily for health reasons, but by feeding the dynamics of network.

4. **Positive and Negative Impacts**:

- **Positive**: For some, visibility on social media can generate **solidarity** and **empathy**, helping to reduce the stigma surrounding mental disorders. Sharing your experiences can be a way to gain support and build social networks that would otherwise be inaccessible.

- **Negative**: On the other hand, excessive exposure on social media can **aggravate symptoms** and create a cycle of **dependence on external validation**, something particularly harmful for individuals with personality or psychotic disorders, who may already have difficulty distinguishing between the real and the virtual.

5. **Conclusion**: The success of **psychotics**, **borderlines** and **schizophrenics** on social networks can be seen as an expression of the contemporary dynamics of networks, which value difference, authenticity and emotional intensity. However, this success can both **amplify** and **exacerbate** the difficulties these people face in everyday life, turning their experiences into entertainment for others, or, in some cases, forms of validation and belonging. The important thing is to understand the impact of this environment on the mental health and construction of these people’s subjectivity.

# Lacanian differences between Frustration, Castration and Deprivation...

Lacanian differences between Frustration, Castration and Deprivation

In Lacanian theory, the concepts of **frustration** , **castration** , and **privation** are crucial to understanding how the subject relates to desire, lack, and the formation of the unconscious. Each concept represents a different way of experiencing **lack** and its effects on the subject's psychological development. Let’s break down the differences between them:1. **Frustration (Frustration)** :Frustration refers to the **absence of the object of desire** —something the subject wants but is not receiving. In Lacan's early teaching, frustration is often related to the **Imaginary order**, where the subject experiences a loss or lack in a more direct or concrete way.

- **Relation to the mother** : In the mother-child relationship, frustration typically arises when the child feels deprived of the **mother's attention or presence** , particularly when the mother holds something the child desires (such as nourishment, love, or care).

- **Role of the Imaginary** : Frustration operates primarily in the **imaginary register** , focusing on **imaginary objects** and **needs** . The child experiences a sense of loss or **demand** for something that is not fulfilled.

- **Effect on the subject** : Frustration is about the **feeling of incompleteness** , but it is not yet tied to the symbolic law or the realm of unconscious desire. It's more about an **unfulfilled need**.

2. **Castration (Castration)** : Castration is central to Lacan's concept of **the Symbolic order** and relates to the subject's encounter with **the Law** —specifically, the **Name-of- the- Father** and the prohibition of incest. Castration refers not to a literal cutting but to the **symbolic loss** that structures desire.

- **Symbolic order** : Castration is associated with the **introduction of the law** and the **acceptance of limits** . It marks the moment when the child understands that **desire must be mediated by the Other** and that they cannot be everything for the mother.

- **Loss of the Phallus** : Castration refers to the **loss of the imaginary Phallus** —the fantasy of being the complete object of the mother’s desire. The subject realizes that neither they nor the mother possess the Phallus, and this acknowledgment structures their entry into the symbolic world of language and social law.

- **Desire and Lack** : Castration is what institutes the **lack** in the subject that gives rise to **desire** . Desire is born out of the recognition that **no one can fully possess the Phallus** . It structures the subject’s relationship to the **Other** and to language itself.

3. **Privation (Privation)** :Privation, unlike frustration and castration, refers to a more **absolute form of lack** —the **lack of an object** that was **never there** to begin with. In this sense, privation does not involve the loss or removal of something, but rather a **pre-existing void** .

- **Lack in the Real** : Privation is related to the **Real** and indicates that there is something fundamentally **missing** in the structure of the subject’s world. The object in question is **absent from the outset** , not simply withheld or taken away.

- **Primordial absence** : Privation involves a more **radical absence** than frustration. While frustration implies that the object of desire was once available and is now missing, privation suggests that this object **was never available** —it was always missing from the subject’s experience.

- **The Other’s lack** : Privation is closely linked to the notion that the **Other is incomplete** —that there is something missing in the Other, which the subject cannot fill. This recognition has profound implications for how the subject navigates desire and its limits.

### Comparison Summary:

- **Frustration** : Involves the **Imaginary** lack of an object or need that the subject desires but is **not receiving** (usually from the mother).

- **Castration** : Is the **Symbolic loss** tied to the introduction of the **Law** (the Name-of-the-Father), where the subject realizes the **Phallus** is not theirs and accepts the limits of desire.

- **Privation** : Is the **Real lack** of something that was **never there** to begin with, a more **absolute absence** .
  Each concept reflects a different way of relating to **lack** and is crucial for understanding how the subject's relationship to **desire** and **the Other** is structured.

# Analysis of getting rich without working, worries and troubles...

Analysis of getting rich without working, worries and troubles

The idea of **getting rich without working** brings up complex psychological, social, and ethical dimensions, often tied to fantasies of **escaping the demands of labor** and achieving effortless success. However, this fantasy is usually accompanied by **worries** and **troubles** that stem from deeper concerns about identity, worth, and social relations. Let's explore these aspects from several angles:1. **The Fantasy of Getting Rich Without Working** :This fantasy often revolves around the desire for **freedom from constraints** , where wealth is seen as the ultimate solution to life’s problems. In Lacanian terms, it could be connected to the **imaginary ideal** of completeness—being free from the **lack** that structures desire.

- **Escape from Lack** : In this fantasy, wealth represents an attempt to bypass the structural **lack** inherent in human life. The belief is that money will provide everything—pleasure, recognition, security—without the need to confront the limitations imposed by reality or social law.

- **The Phallus and Power** : Wealth is often imagined as a form of the **Phallus** , symbolizing power and control. In this case, getting rich without working might be seen as a way to possess the **ultimate symbol** of success and value in the world without going through the usual channels of effort and competition.

- **Wish for Autonomy** : Behind this fantasy is also the desire for **autonomy** —to be free from dependence on others and to exist outside the demands of the social order. This is often related to the **frustration** of feeling bound by the need to work or conform to societal expectations.

2. **Worries and Troubles: Internal Conflicts** :
   Despite the allure of effortless wealth, the fantasy can give rise to a series of psychological and emotional conflicts.

- **Fear of Emptiness** : Achieving wealth without working might lead to a sense of **emptiness** or **lack of purpose** . If one’s identity is tied to work or productivity, suddenly acquiring wealth can destabilize that sense of self. The absence of work can create a **void** where meaning and fulfillment used to be found.

- **Guilt and Shame** : Many people experience **guilt** or **shame** if they gain wealth without effort, especially in cultures that value hard work and merit. The feeling of **undeserved success** can lead to internal conflicts, questioning the legitimacy of one's achievements.

- **Loss of Meaning** : Work, in many ways, provides a structure for life and a sense of **purpose** . Without it, even wealth may fail to provide **satisfaction** . This can result in a deep existential **crisis** , where the person questions what they are living for if everything comes too easily.

3. **Social Relations and Isolation** :Becoming rich without working can also impact one’s relationships with others, as wealth and success can create **divisions** and **jealousies** .

- **Alienation from Others** : There may be a sense of **alienation** or disconnection from friends, family, or peers who continue to work hard. People may feel **resentment** or **envy** , creating tension and social distance. The wealthy individual might also struggle to trust others, worrying that relationships are based on money rather than genuine connection.

- **Loss of Social Identity** : In many societies, a person's **work** or **career** plays a central role in defining their **social identity** . Without this, individuals might feel adrift, unsure of their place in the social order. **Status anxiety** can also emerge if the person feels that they do not "deserve" their wealth in the eyes of others.

4. **The Burden of Managing Wealth** :Ironically, getting rich without working can create **new forms of labor** and anxiety. Managing wealth, especially large sums, often involves complex decisions, responsibilities, and even **fears of loss** .

- **Fear of Losing Wealth** : The fear of losing unearned wealth can be a source of constant worry. Without the experience of building wealth through effort, there may be a lack of **confidence** in managing it, leading to anxieties about **investment** choices, **taxes** , or **financial security** .

- **Pressure to Maintain Status** : With wealth comes an implicit pressure to maintain or even grow it. This can create stress, especially if the wealth was not earned through direct work, leaving the individual feeling uncertain about how to handle this newfound responsibility.

5. **Ethical and Moral Dimensions** :There is often a **moral conflict** embedded in the idea of wealth without work, as it challenges traditional values of **effort** and **fairness** .

- **Moral Judgments** : In many societies, there is a cultural expectation that wealth should be the result of **hard work** and **merit** . Getting rich without working can create a moral dilemma, where the individual may feel judged by others or even judge themselves for not having "earned" their success.

- **Responsibility to Others** : Some may feel a sense of **responsibility** to redistribute their wealth or use it for the good of others, but this can also create pressure and anxiety over how to properly manage this responsibility. **Philanthropy** or other forms of social engagement might emerge as a way to resolve this internal conflict.

### Conclusion:

The fantasy of **getting rich without working** taps into deep psychological desires for **freedom** , **power** , and **autonomy** , but it also brings with it a host of **worries** and **troubles** . These include internal conflicts about **identity** and **purpose**, feelings of **guilt** or **shame**, potential **alienation** from others, and new anxieties related to managing wealth. Ultimately, wealth without work can expose the limits of **material success** in providing lasting **fulfillment**, highlighting the complex relationship between desire, lack, and the social order.

# Homosexual desires in criminality, sublimation of criminality: fantasies of fighting, physical contact...

Homosexual desires in criminality, sublimation of criminality: fantasies of fighting, physical contactThis content may violate our [usage policies](https://openai.com /policies/usage-policies) .

**Homosexual desires** in criminality and the sublimation of this desire through violence or criminality can be understood from different perspectives, especially within psychoanalysis. Here, we can explore how **fantasies** fighting**, **physical contact** and violent behavior may be related to repressed or unconscious desires, and how these desires may be **sublimated** or displaced into actions apparently unrelated to their sexual origin.1. **Criminality and Repressed Desires**: In psychoanalysis, **repressed homosexual desires** can emerge in ways not recognized by the subject, often through behaviors involving aggression or physical confrontation. Violence can function as a space of **projection** or **displaced expression\*\* of a desire that does not find its direct form of realization.

- **Fights and Physical Contact**: In environments such as crime, physical violence and fights can symbolically represent a form of **body contact** that carries an erotic charge, even if it is not recognized as such. Physical fighting can become a way of realizing **fantasies of bodily closeness**, which, due to repression, do not manifest themselves in a directly sexual way.

- **Rivalry and Desire**: **Rivalry** with another man, which can culminate in violence, often carries unresolved homosexual subtexts. In a fight, the intense physical contact, excitement and tension that accompany these confrontations can be a veiled manifestation of **desire**.

2. **Sublimation of Criminality**: **Sublimation** is the psychoanalytic process by which in which a desire that cannot be directly fulfilled is diverted into more socially acceptable activities, or in some cases, into activities that mask the true underlying drive. In the case of criminality, violent or criminal acts may be a **sublimation** of unfulfilled sexual desires.

- **Aggression as Sublimation**: Violence and crime can function as a way to **sublimate** homosexual desires, offering the subject a way to express his desire through behaviors that, at first At first glance, they do not seem sexual. By channeling these desires into criminal or aggressive actions, the subject may experience a release of emotional and bodily tension that might otherwise be directed toward a **sexual encounter**.

- **Identification and Projection**: The aggressor may project onto his victims certain attributes that he himself represses or with which he unconsciously identifies. For example, **humiliation** or **dominance** over the other may serve as a way of trying to dominate or reject one's own repressed desire.

3. **Fantasies of Fighting and the Eroticism of Violence**: The use of physical violence in criminal fantasies can have a strong erotic connotation, even if this is unconscious. For some individuals, **physical fighting** represents an intense form of physical contact, which can beperceived as a fulfillment, albeit deformed, of a **homosexual desire**.

- **Excitement and Aggression**: For some individuals, physical contact in violence brings an **excitement** that is close to sexual arousal. The act of fighting, touching the other's body with force and intensity, can carry a repressed erotic component, in which aggression is mixed with desire. This type of **fighting fantasy** can manifest itself in contexts where **vulnerability** and **physical proximity** are seen as threatening to traditional masculine identity.

- **Sublimation in Sports and Crime**: Sports can often function as a form of **sublimation** of this desire, where controlled violence allows physical contact between men to occur within a socially accepted context. However, when sublimated into crime, this desire can take on dangerous forms, such as the search for physical confrontations to "resolve" the underlying sexual tension. 4. **Criminality as an Attempt to Resist Vulnerability**: In some cases, criminality and violence can be attempts to **resist** the experience of **vulnerability** that homosexual desire could imply for the subject. Aggression can be a way of denying or **repressing** this desire, trying to position oneself as the dominant or active one in a relationship that, otherwise, could be seen as passive or submissive.
- **Masculinity and Criminality**: In many cultures, criminality is closely linked to ideas of **masculinity** and **power**. Homosexual desire, perceived as a threat to this masculinity, can be displaced into aggression as a way of reaffirming one's own masculinity. Involvement in criminal and violent activities can thus be seen as an **attempt to reaffirm one's masculine identity**, avoiding the threat of homosexual desire. 5. **Conclusion**: **Homosexual desires** sublimated into criminality, especially through fantasies of fighting and physical contact, reflect a **complex psychic dynamic** where repressed desire finds expression through indirect means. Crime and violence can serve as **paths of sublimation**, where the subject discharges unconscious tensions, without directly confronting the sexual nature of his impulses. These manifestations reveal how desire can shift into **socially destructive forms**, when it does not find appropriate channels of recognition or acceptance. Violence and criminality, in this context, can be interpreted as **failed attempts to deal with desire**, creating a cycle of destruction and suffering both for the individual and for the people around him.

# Master and slave dialectic, struggle for pure prestige, father for death, mother for slavery...

Master and slave dialectic, struggle for pure prestige, father for death, mother for slavery

The **master and slave dialectic**, originating in the work of Hegel, is a dialectical process that explores the dynamics of recognition and power in human relationships. In its most basic form, this dialectic portrays the struggle between two subjects who confront each other to obtain recognition from each other, resulting in the subjugation of one and the rise of the other. This conflict reveals deep tensions about the **struggle for prestige**, the desire for **recognition**, and the consequences of this for the formation of consciousness and social identities.
Applying these concepts to the relationship between father, mother and children in the family structure, the idea of ​​"father for death" and "mother for slavery" suggests an archetypal division of roles and powers that can be symbolically associated with these figures.

1. **Dialectics of Master and Slave (Hegel)**:
   The dialectic of master and slave is a landmark of Hegelian thought that involves two consciousnesses that come into conflict due to the need to be recognized as autonomous.

- **Struggle forPrestige link**: Each of the two subjects seeks to be recognized as superior, desiring recognition from the other without giving recognition in return. This "struggle of pure prestige" (as conceived by Alexandre Kojève, an important interpreter of Hegel) involves a confrontation for domination and independence. The one who is willing to risk his life in the struggle becomes the **master**, while the other, by retreating for fear of death, assumes the role of **slave**.

- **Role of the Slave**: The slave, although subjugated, is transformed by the experience of work. By working for the master, the slave develops a relationship with the material world and, through this work, gains a form of independence and self-knowledge that the master, in his position of dominance, cannot obtain.

- **Recognition and Autonomy**: The dialectical relationship between master and slave suggests that true recognition can only be obtained through reciprocity, that is, the complete subjugation of the other denies full recognition, since the slave is no longer an autonomous consciousness capable of offering genuine recognition.

2. **Father to Death**: The expression "father to death" can be interpreted as associated with the **symbolic role** of the father in structuring law and order, especially from a psychoanalytic perspective.

- **Paternal Power and Authority**: In the family imagination, the father is often seen as the representative of the **law**, the one who imposes limits and discipline, guiding the child out of the symbolic fusion with the mother and introducing him into the social order. This can be interpreted as a "death" of the phase of pure fusion with the mother, where the father acts as an agent of separation and socialization.

- **Relationship with Death and Destiny**: The figure of the father can also be associated with the idea of ​​**destiny** and **mortality**. In many cultures, the father is the bearer of the name and lineage, and also the one who prepares the child to face the reality of death and finitude, a process of facing reality that can be experienced as a rupture or "death" of a more primary form of existence.

3. **Mother for Slavery**: The idea of ​​"mother for slavery" may reflect the view that, in the family dialectic, the mother occupies a place of **service** and **sacrifice**, often linked to the care and maintenance of life, in a way that approaches the condition of "slave" in the context of family dynamics.

- **Slavery in Care**: The mother, traditionally, is seen as the figure who sacrifices herself for the children, taking care of their needs and often renouncing her own autonomy and desires. This role of caregiver can be read symbolically as a form of "slavery", in the sense that she is totally tied to the other (the children), without the possibility of separating herself.

- **Dependence and Service**: The figure of the mother, within this context, can be placed in a position of dependence on the desire of the child and the needs of the family, which reflects a form of **subjugation** within the traditional family structure.

4. **Family Dialectics and Psychoanalysis**: In psychoanalysis, especially in the Lacanian reading of the family structure, the relationship between father, mother and child is structured in such a way as to reflect the tensions between **law**, **desire** and **lack**.

- **Father and the Symbolic Law**: The father represents the **Symbolic Law** that separates the child from the mother, a cut that introduces the subject into the field of **desire** and the **symbolic**. This cut can be experienced as a form of **castration**, where the father interrupts the relationship of completeness that the child has with the mother.

- **Mother and Desire**: The mother, on the other hand, is often seen as the primary object of the child's desire, and her position can be experienced by the child as being both loving and servitude, especially as the mother appears to be available to meet the child's needs, but also maintains her own desires that go beyond the child's desire.beyond the child.

5. **Struggle for Prestige and Recognition in the Family Context**: Within the family microcosm, the **struggle for recognition** and prestige can manifest itself in complex ways, where the roles of master and slave are distributed between father, mother and children.

- **Father and Prestige**: The father seeks recognition of authority, both from the mother and from the children, while the mother may, in her position as caregiver, find herself in a position of dependence that resonates with the role of the slave in Hegelian dialectics.

- **Mother and Prestige**: The mother, although placed in a role of care and "slavery", also occupies a position of symbolic power, being the first object of the child's desire and the center of his first affective experiences. The mother's prestige is often veiled or silent, but equally powerful.

### Conclusion:

The **master and slave dialectic** provides a powerful tool for understanding the dynamics of **prestige**, **authority** and **recognition** in the family context. The figure of the **father** can be associated with **death** in the symbolic sense of separation and imposition of law, while the **mother** can be seen as related to **slavery** in the sense of her role as a caregiver subjugated to the desire of the other. These metaphors reveal the complex negotiations of power and desire that permeate human relationships, both in the intimate and social spheres.
