- **Other discourse**: It refers to otherness, to what is beyond the subject's understanding. It is the position that recognizes and deals with the limit of the symbolic and the presence of the Real. This discourse can be associated with the field of the feminine or with a position of openness to the desire of the Other.

- **Woman's discourse**: For Lacan, **“Woman does not exist”** as a universal category, since the feminine is multiple and marked by the relationship with the **Other's enjoyment**. The woman's discourse would be linked to openness to difference, to the lack of completeness and to the relationship with love as an attempt to deal with lack.

- **Discourse of lack**: Articulates the relationship with castration, that is, the acceptance that desire arises from incompleteness. In this discourse, the subject recognizes lack as constitutive, which can lead to a position of greater openness to the Other and the renunciation of illusions of phallic completeness.

- **Discourse of care**: Related to an ethics of care for the Other. It is a discourse that recognizes fragility and vulnerability as part of the human condition. It can be thought of as a response to the phallic/provider discourse, focusing on interdependence and the need for symbolic and material support.

---

**Interactions and Contrapositions**
These discourses are not autonomous, but interact and often come into conflict:

- The **phallic discourse** tends to confront the **woman's discourse**, as the latter subverts the logic of phallic completeness.

- The **provider discourse** can be challenged by the **lack discourse**, by questioning the idealization of provision as a solution to desire.

- The **discourseThe imperialist bear** is often destabilized by the **discourse of care**, which points to the importance of vulnerability and interdependence.

---

**Clinic and Ethics**
In the psychoanalytic clinic, exploring these discourses helps the subject to recognize their subjective position and the narratives that support their relationship with the Other and desire. Psychoanalysis seeks to decenter the subject from fixed positions (such as those of the phallic or imperialist discourse) and open space for new symbolic articulations, which include lack and otherness.

For example:

- A subject fixated on the **discourse of the provider** may suffer from the impossibility of fully sustaining the desire of the Other, leading to anguish.

- A subject in the **discourse of lack** may learn to find new forms of enjoyment that do not depend on the fantasy of completeness.

This reflection allows us to address the complexity of human relationships and the plurality of subjective positions in relation to desire, enjoyment and language.

Hegel, the Slave and the Refusal of Profit, Honest Poor People Who Abhor Profit
The Traumatic Effect of Profit, Surplus Value, Surplus Enjoyment

# The analysis of the refusal of profit, especially in contexts of poverty or ethical positions that abhor the idea of ​​enrichment, can be enriched by the intersection between Hegel's dialectic, the Marxist concept of **surplus value** and Lacanian psychoanalysis, particularly the concept of **surplus enjoyment**.

---

**1. Hegel and the Dialectic of Master and Slave**
In Hegel's philosophy, the relationship between the Master and the Slave is central to understanding the constitution of subjectivity. In this dialectic:

- The **Master** seeks recognition through domination over the Other. However, he depends on the Slave's work to sustain his enjoyment and social position.

- The **Slave**, on the other hand, finds a form of freedom in work and in the transformation of matter, even while submissive. Through work, the Slave achieves a mediated relationship with the world and desire.

This dynamic can be applied to understanding how some people refuse profit. Profit, associated with the position of Master, is seen as something that perpetuates exploitation, alienation and symbolic violence against the Other. Thus, an ethical position that abhors profit can be understood as an attempt to refuse this logic of domination and alienation.

---

**2. Marx: Surplus Value and Alienation** Marx's critique of capitalism articulates the concept of **surplus value**, which is the surplus value produced by the labor of the proletariat and appropriated by the capitalist. This surplus is the basis of profit.

- Profit, for Marx, is traumatic because it represents the **exploitation of the labor of others** and the alienation of the worker, who does not recognize himself in the product of his labor.

- The refusal of profit by "honest poor" people can be read as a rejection of this exploitation. However, this position often leaves the subject trapped in a logic of submission, since he rejects the gain but remains subject to the system that exploits him.

---

**3. Lacan: Plus-de-Joy and the Traumatics of Profit** In Lacanian psychoanalysis, the concept of **plus-de-jouir** (or _plus-de-jouir_) is closely linked to the idea of ​​an excess of enjoyment, which is generated from lack and desire, but which is structurally impossible to be completely satisfied.

- Profit can be traumatic because it refers to the **Real of enjoyment**, that is, to the impossibility of complete satisfaction. Earning or accumulating wealth can expose the subject to an existential void that no amount of profit can fill.

- For some people, profit can be seen as something indecent or "impure", as it represents the **enjoyment of the Other**, that is, something that goes beyond the ethical or subjective limits of what would be acceptable. This rejection of profit may be linked to an attempt to avoid confronting one's own excessive enjoyment.

--

**4. The "Honest Poor Man" and the Enjoyment of Renunciation**
The figure of the "honest poor man" who abhors profit can be analyzed as a subjective position supported by the ethics of sacrifice:

- By refusing profit, the subject positions himself as someone who preserves his **symbolic purity**, not giving in to the logic of capitalism.

- However, this position can hide an **enjoyment in renunciation**, that is, an unconscious pleasure in not enjoying, in avoiding the material or symbolic satisfaction that profit could provide.

---

**5. Interfaces: Profit Trauma and Social Structure**
The relationship with profit can also be traumatic because it exposes the subject to ethical and existential conflicts:

- **Enjoyment of the Other**: Profit is perceived as something that belongs to the Other (the capitalist, the system), and accepting it can be experienced as a submission to the enjoyment of the Other.

- **Guilt**: In many cultures and religious traditions, profit is associated with greed, which can generate guilt in the subject when benefiting from it.

- **Alienation**: Profit is seen as a result of the exploitation of labor, making it something distant from the subject and his/her own creative capacity.

---

**Conclusion: Ethics, Profit and Psychoanalysis**

The refusal of profit, when seen through the lenses of Hegel, Marx and Lacan, is a position that reveals tensions between ethics, desire and enjoyment.

- While Hegel shows us that this refusal can be a resistance to the logic of domination,

- Marx highlights how it reflects the alienation of the capitalist system, and

- Lacan helps us understand the unconscious enjoyment that sustains this position, whether in excess or in renunciation.

The challenge is to find ways of dealing with the trauma of profit that do not fall into paralyzing positions, but that allow the subject to rewrite his/her relationship with desire, enjoyment and the symbolic economy of the Other.

Modern woman, businesswoman, motherhood as a business, business with children, meetings, business between parties

# The figure of the modern and business woman, in conjunction with motherhood, can be analyzed as a contemporary phenomenon that subverts or reformulates the traditional roles attributed to women. This analysis, inspired by Lacanian psychoanalysis, allows us to understand motherhood as a field of negotiations and the child as a central element in this scenario.

---

**1. Modern Woman and Businesswoman**
The modern woman places herself in a place that goes beyond the traditional boundaries of femininity:

- **Autonomy and production**: The modern woman assumes productive and active roles in society, especially in the job market.

- **The enjoyment of doing**: In psychoanalysis, the woman's relationship with work can be read as an investment in a field of her own enjoyment, which is not necessarily aligned with phallic enjoyment.

- **Business as a symbolic space**: The business world involves decisions, exchanges and contracts, in which women are involved as desiring subjects and active agents.

---

**2. Motherhood as a Business**

In the contemporary context, motherhood is often mediated by logics that evoke the business field:

- **Child as a "project"**: Raising children is often organized as an enterprise, with financial, educational and social planning.

- **The maternal function and the social Other**: The modern mother constantly negotiates between the demands of the Other (society, culture, market) and her own desires.

- **Symbolic investment**: Motherhood can be seen as an investment in symbolic transmission, but also in social, emotional and even material returns.

---

**3. Business with the Child**
In psychoanalysis, the child occupies a fundamental place in maternal discourse:

- **Object of exchange**: The child can be an object of symbolic negotiation between the mother and the father, or between the mother and the social Other.

- **Child as an extension of desire**: In modern times, the child is often seen asa reflection of the parents' success, being projected as an investment "product".

- **The enjoyment of care**: The relationship with the child can also involve maternal enjoyment, which is realized in the role of caring, planning and protecting.

---

**4. Meeting and Business between Parties**
Motherhood and business can be found in dynamics that involve:

- **Family meetings**: The organization of family life can take on the character of a company, with defined roles, goals and negotiations between parties.

- **Symbolic contract**: The relationship between mother, father and children can be understood as a contractual relationship, in which there are shared expectations and responsibilities.

- **Subjectivity in the market**: The modern woman, as a mother and professional, negotiates her subjective position between the fields of production (market) and reproduction (family).

---

**5. Lacan and the Dimension of Desire and Enjoyment**

- **Maternal desire**: For Lacan, the mother's desire organizes the child's entry into the symbolic field. However, in modernity, this desire can be contaminated by market and social demands.

- **Phallic enjoyment and other enjoyment**: The modern woman moves between phallic enjoyment (production, success, power) and the other enjoyment, which escapes phallic norms and is related to the dimension of care and creation.

- **The object a in the child**: The child can occupy the place of object a for the mother, being at the same time that which causes her desire and that which keeps her in an active position in the symbolic field.

---

**6. Conclusion**
The modern businesswoman and mother exemplifies how traditional roles are being transformed by contemporary times. Motherhood, far from being just a natural or biological function, becomes a space for negotiations:

- Negotiations between personal desire and social demands.

- Between the enjoyment of professional achievement and the enjoyment of the relationship with children.

- Between the symbolic and the imaginary in the place occupied by the child.

Psychoanalysis helps us understand how these transformations reveal new forms of enjoyment and subjective organization, but also new challenges for the subject in the field of desire and identity.

Separation from the Other, the Other has abandoned me, it is okay to be separated

# Separation from the Other is a central theme in psychoanalysis, especially in the work of Lacan, who articulates ...

Separation from the Other is a central theme in psychoanalysis, especially in the work of Lacan, who articulates the relationship of the subject with the Other as structuring desire, lack and subjective constitution. The idea that "the Other has abandoned me" and the possibility of accepting separation touch on crucial questions about desire, enjoyment and subjective autonomy.

---

**1. Separation from the Other**

- **The Other in psychoanalysis** : For Lacan, the Other (with a capital "O") is a symbolic instance that represents language, law, culture and the otherness that organizes the subject.

- **Structural separation** : From the beginning, separation from the Other is necessary for the subject to constitute himself as a desiring person. The initial symbiotic relationship with the Other (generally represented by the mother) must be broken so that the subject can inscribe himself in the field of desire.

- **The role of the Name-of-the-Father** : Separation from the primordial Other occurs through the paternal function, which blocks the subject's desire in relation to the mother and introduces the symbolic law.

--- **2. "The Other has abandoned me"**

- **Anguish and loss** : When the subject feels that the Other has abandoned him, anguish can arise, linked to the loss of the symbolic guarantee that the Other provides.

- **Lack in the Other**: For Lacan, the Other is not complete, but also has a lack. Recognizing this lack is an essential step for the subject to accept his own condition as a desiring individual.

- **Fantasy of completeness**: The idea that the Other should fulfill all the subject's needs is a fantasy. The abandonment of the Other, in this sense, can be experienced as an opportunity to overcome this illusion.

---

**3. Accepting Separation**- **Subjective growth**: Acceptance of separation from the Other is a milestone in psychic maturity. It allows the subject to deal with the lack without demanding a definitive response from the Other.

- **Autonomy and desire**: With separation, the subject can sustain his/her desire autonomously, without depending on the desire or approval of the Other.

- **The place of enjoyment**: Separation from the Other can allow the subject to find forms of enjoyment that do not depend directly on the presence or validation of the Other.

---

**4. Clinical Implications** - **The suffering of abandonment**: In the clinic, the feeling of abandonment by the Other can appear as melancholy, complaint or resistance to change. Working on separation means helping the analysand to deal with his/her lack and that of the Other.

- **Symbolic reconstruction**: Analysis can help the subject to reconfigure his/her relationship with the Other, recognizing separation as a structural and necessary condition.

- **Desire as a motor**: Separation allows desire to function as a motor of psychic life, instead of paralyzing the subject in a symbiotic bond with the Other.

---

**5. "It's okay to be separated"**

- **Subjective freedom**: Recognizing that separation is part of the human condition is liberating. It allows the subject to abandon unrealistic demands in relation to the Other and to accept his own lack.

- **Transformation of the bond**: Separation does not mean the absence of a bond, but a transformation in the way the subject relates to the Other and to his own desire.

- **Creating new meanings**: Separation can be a creative moment, where the subject reinscribes his position in the symbolic field and creates new meanings for his existence.

---

**6. Conclusion**
Separation from the Other is not only an experience of loss, but an essential condition for the constitution of the subject and for the functioning of desire. Accepting this separation is an ethical gesture, which implies renouncing the fantasy of completeness and opening up to new possibilities of subjectivation and relationship with the world.

Choices for the end of analysis, crossing fantasy, the analyst's desire

# **Choices for the End of Analysis, Crossing Fantasy and the Analyst's Desire** ...

**Choices for the End of Analysis, Crossing Fantasy and the Analyst's Desire**
In the course of Lacanian psychoanalysis, the end of analysis, the crossing of fantasy and the analyst's desire are fundamental concepts that are interrelated, marking an ethical process of subjective transformation.

---

**1. End of Analysis**

- **Redefinition of the subject** : The end of analysis is a point where the subject recognizes his/her position in relation to desire and jouissance. It is not about "healing" in the medical sense, but about operating a structural change in the way the subject relates to his/her lack and his/her symptom.

- **Identification with the symptom**: At the end of the analysis, the subject does not eliminate the symptom, but identifies with it in a way that allows him to deal with his discomfort without alienation. This identification is also the acceptance of the singular way in which the subject enjoys.

- **Production of knowledge**: The analysand builds knowledge about his unconscious, about the conditions of his entry into desire and about the impasses that marked his history. This knowledge is transmissible to the analyst and, eventually, to the psychoanalytic community.

---

**2. Crossing Fantasy** - **The function of fantasy**: Fantasy is the imaginary structure that supports the subject's desire. It is the way in which the subject tries to deal with the lack of the Other and the unbearability of the Real.

- **What does it mean to cross fantasy?** - Crossing fantasy implies disalienating oneself from the position that the subject occupies in the desire of the Other.

- The subject realizes that desire is not determined by the Other, but that he himself is responsible for his desiring position.

- **The Real after fantasy**: After crossing the fantasy, the subject is confronted with the Real, that is, with that which escapes symbolization. At that moment, desire begins to be experienced in aless illusory and freer form.

---

**3. The Analyst's Desire**
---**The role of the analyst**: The analyst's desire is not a desire in the usual sense, but an ethical position. It is sustained by unconscious knowledge and by the search to enable the analysand to construct his own desiring position.

---**"Being the object a"**: The analyst operates as an incarnation of the object a, placing himself as the cause of the analysand's desire. This position is what allows the subject to face his fantasies and the Real that they cover.

---**Desire as a cause and not as a demand**: The analyst's desire is not directed at responding to the analysand's demands, but at sustaining the place of desire as the driving force of the analysis.

---**4. Choices at the End of Analysis**
---**Ethics of desire**: At the end of analysis, the subject makes ethical choices in relation to his desire. This may mean breaking with certain fantasies or redefining his goals in life.

- **Relationship with enjoyment**: The choices made at the end of analysis include the way in which the subject deals with his/her enjoyment. He/she may find a less mortifying relationship with enjoyment, which does not imply the compulsive repetition of certain impasses.

- **Openness to the new**: The end of analysis does not imply the resolution of all problems, but a new way of being in the world, more open and less alienated.

---

**5. Interrelations**

- **Crossing and end of analysis**: Crossing fantasy is one of the conditions for the end of analysis. By crossing fantasy, the subject is able to redefine his/her relationship with desire and enjoyment.

- **Analyst's desire and end of analysis**: The analyst's desire sustains the possibility of the end of analysis. Without it, the analysand may remain trapped in his/her demands and not advance in the crossing.

- **Unconscious knowledge**: Analysis culminates in the construction of knowledge about the unconscious, which implies both the crossing of fantasy and the possibility of occupying a new ethical position in relation to desire.

---

**6. Conclusion**
The end of analysis in Lacan is not a "cure" in the classical sense, but an ethical change that allows the subject to reposition himself in relation to desire, jouissance and the Other. The crossing of fantasy and the desire of the analyst are fundamental in this process, allowing the analysand to free himself from the alienations that imprisoned him and, at the same time, to build a new relationship with his unconscious and with the world.

Men with male fantasies
He is stabilized in his position as a man, he is a man and likes to be a man, he acts like a man
Even with a trans woman who looks like a man

# This issue can be addressed in light of Lacanian psychoanalysis considering the dynamics of desire, fantasies and identifications that structure the subject.

**1. Stabilization in the Position of Man**

- **Symbolic identification:** The man who is "stabilized" in his position as a man reveals a clear and solid identification with the symbolic signifier "man". He inserts himself in the phallic register as the one who recognizes himself in the signifier that organizes his position in desire and in the relationship with the Other.

- **Male enjoyment:** Lacan differentiates the modes of male and female enjoyment. The man who identifies as a man and "enjoys being a man" aligns himself with phallic enjoyment, which is structured around the logic of completeness and the desire to dominate the lack in the Other.
  **2. Fantasy and Desire**
- **The structure of fantasy:** Fantasy is the imaginary framework that organizes the subject's desire. Male fantasies are often linked to the phallus as a central element, whether in the search for possession or recognition of this phallus in the Other.

- **Desire as a relationship with lack:** Even with a trans woman who looks like a man, what is at stake in the subject's desire is not the anatomical reality of the partner, but how this person articulates himself within his fantasy.
  **3. Relationship with a Trans Woman whoLooks Like a Man**
- **The question of semblance:** In Lacan, semblance is what covers up the lack and organizes the relationship with the Other. The masculine appearance of a trans woman can function as a semblance that mobilizes masculine desire without shaking the subject's identification as a man.

- **Enjoyment in the encounter:** This subject can find in the trans body a mode of enjoyment that is articulated with his own fantasmatic structure. The relationship does not challenge his masculine identification because desire operates in the symbolic and imaginary field, not at the biological level.

**4. Stability in the Symbolic Register**

- **The relationship with the Other:** The "stabilization" as a man reflects a symbolic anchoring in the phallic signifier. This does not imply rigidity, but rather an ability to sustain one's subjective position even in the face of situations that could be seen as destabilizing by a normative logic.

- **The inclusion of the Other in desire:** The desire of a man who is "stabilized" in his masculine position can include different forms of enjoyment and relationship with the Other, without this calling into question his subjective position.

---

**Conclusion**
The situation described is an example of how desire and fantasy transcend biological and normative categories. The "stabilized" man as such is not defined only by the anatomy or behavior of his partner, but by the way he organizes his desire in the symbolic and imaginary. The relationship with a trans woman who looks like a man can be inscribed without contradiction in his fantasy, as long as it is in line with the logic of enjoyment that structures his subjectivity.

Perversion and Neurosis, perverse fantasy and neurotic fantasy analysis with the Klein Bottle

# **Perversion and Neurosis in Lacan** Lacan differentiates **neurosis** and **perversion** mainly by...

**Perversion and Neurosis in Lacan** Lacan differentiates **neurosis** and **perversion** mainly by their relationship with desire and the Other. These two modes of subjective structuring are defined by the subject's position in relation to lack, desire and jouissance.

- **Neurosis:** The subject maintains a relationship mediated by fantasy with the desire of the Other, sustaining the lack as a central point of his subjectivity.

- **Perversion:** The subject positions himself as an agent of the Other's jouissance, refusing the lack and positioning himself as the one who "knows" what the Other desires, instead of submitting to the uncertainty of desire.

---

**The Klein Bottle: Topological Structure** The **Klein Bottle** is a topological surface that illustrates the continuity between interior and exterior. In Lacan, this figure is used to think about the relationship between **inside and outside** in the field of desire and enjoyment, especially in psychic structures.**1. Neurosis and the Klein Bottle**

In neurosis, there is a constant attempt to delimit the inside and the outside, that is, to mark where the subject begins and where the Other begins. However, this delimitation is never fully successful, which keeps the subject trapped in the logic of lack and the search for meaning.

- **Neurotic fantasy:** Fantasy functions as a border that organizes desire, supporting the idea that enjoyment is inaccessible.

- **Relationship with the Klein Bottle:** The neurotic tries to build a safe "inside" for himself, but is constantly invaded by the "outside" (the desire of the Other), which threatens his stability. The topology of the Klein Bottle illustrates how what seemed to be "outside" returns to the inside, symbolizing the impossibility of completely separating the subject from the Other.

**2. Perversion and the Klein Bottle**
In perversion, the subject refuses the separation between inside and outside, placing himself directly in the place of the Other's enjoyment. He actively manipulates enjoyment, denying the lack that characterizes the Other's desire.

- **Perverse fantasy:** The perverse fantasy operates in such a way as to erase the border between inside and outside, affirming a continuity that nullifies the lack.

- **Relationship with the Klein Bottle:**For the pervert, the Klein Bottle symbolizes the total continuity between subject and Other. There is no barrier or border, but a direct fusion between interior and exterior. This allows the subject to position himself as an object of enjoyment for the Other.

---

| **Comparison between Neurotic Fantasy and Perverse Fantasy** | Aspect                                            | Neurosis                                                | Perversion |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------- | ---------- |
| Position of the subject                                      | In search of deciphering the desire of the Other  | Positions himself as an object that satisfies the Other |
| Relationship with the lack                                   | Recognizes and suffers the lack                   | Refuses or instrumentalizes the lack of the Other       |
| Enjoyment                                                    | Prevented or indirectly accessed                  | Positioned as the center of enjoyment                   |
| Klein Bottle                                                 | There is an attempt to delimit inside and outside | There is no separation between inside and outside       |

---

**Clinical Implications**

1. **In Neurosis:**

- The clinical work involves exploring how fantasy organizes desire and how the boundary between subject and Other can be elaborated to sustain desire in a less distressing way.

- The Klein Bottle helps to illustrate to the analysand that the "outside" is never totally separated from the "inside", that is, the desire of the Other is always implied in the subject's subjectivity.

2. **In Perversion:**

- The treatment aims to work on the refusal of lack and the direct relationship with the Other's enjoyment.

- The Klein Bottle can be used to show the perverse subject that this continuity between inside and outside is not a complete solution, but an illusion that sustains his or her defensive position.

---

The Klein Bottle, as a topological tool, allows the analyst and the analysand to think in a more visual and concrete way about the complex subjective dynamics of neurosis and perversion, especially in relation to fantasy and enjoyment.

From hysteria to perversion according to the partner's response/demand

If someone demands something that she does not have: she exposes the lack, causing discomfort

If someone refuses her demand for enjoyment, she exposes the lack, causing discomfort

# The transition from **hysteria to perversion** can be analyzed by considering how the subject responds to the d...

The transition from **hysteria to perversion** can be analyzed by considering how the subject responds to the Other's demand and how this interaction touches on the issue of **lack**. In both hysteria and perversion, the relationship with lack is central, but the way of dealing with it is different.**Hysteria and the Response to the Other's Demand** In hysteria, the subject positions himself as the one who questions the Other's desire, that is, continually seeks to decipher what the Other wants from him. This position is structured around **lack**, which appears as something that needs to be filled or responded to.

1. **When the Other demands something that she does not have:**

- Hysteria makes her own lack her "playing field". The subject may become distressed when feeling exposed as "incomplete".

- This discomfort arises because the Other's demand shows that she does not have what she believes the Other desires, reaffirming her position as subject to lack.

2. **When the Other refuses her demand for enjoyment:**

- The Other's refusal is experienced as frustration and can provoke a crisis.

- Hysteria transforms this frustration into a continuous questioning: "What is missing in me for him to desire or recognize me?"

The discomfort of the hysterical subject is, therefore, linked to the constant confrontation with lack, which is perceived as something that cannot be overcome. Her relationship with the Other's desire is mediated by fantasy, which organizes the search for recognition and love.

---

**Perversion and the Response to the Other's Demand** In perversion, the subject responds to the Other's lack in a different way, denying it or instrumentalizing it. The pervert positions himself as the one who **knows what the Other wants**, assuming an active role in the dynamic.

1. **When the Other demands something that he does not have:**

- The pervert denies his own lack and presents himself as capable of fulfilling the Other's desire.
- He acts to manipulate the Other's lack, positioning himself as the one who can fill this absenceor provoke the enjoyment of the Other.

2. **When the Other refuses his demand for enjoyment:**

- The refusal can be experienced as a challenge. Instead of suffering like the hysterical, the pervert transforms the refusal into an opportunity to reaffirm his position of power over the desire of the Other.

- By instrumentalizing the lack, the perverse subject seeks to control the enjoyment of the Other, often ignoring or challenging the imposed limits.

The malaise of the pervert is less linked to the lack and more to the limit of enjoyment, which he constantly tries to surpass or circumvent.

---

| **Essential Difference: The Response to the Lack** | Aspect                                                       | Hysteria                                           | Perversion |
| -------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------- | ---------- |
| Response to the demand of the Other                | Questions: "What does the Other want from me?"               | Affirms: "I know what the Other wants."            |
| Relationship with the lack                         | Suffers the lack, seeks to decipher it                       | Refuses or instrumentalizes the lack               |
| Discomfort                                         | Anguish in the face of the impossibility of filling the lack | Frustration in the face of the limits of enjoyment |

---

**From Hysteria to Perversion: The Role of the Other's Demand** The transition from hysteria to perversion can be understood as a **change in the subjective strategy in the face of the lack and the demand of the Other**. When the subject realizes that he cannot sustain the game of lack (of hysteria), he can start to deny the lack (of perversion), assuming a more active and manipulative position.

1. **If the Other demands something that the subject does not have:**

- Hysteria can transform the lack into anguish and symptom.

- Perversion transforms the lack into a strategy of control over the desire of the Other.

2. **If the Other refuses the subject's demand for enjoyment:**

- The hysteric can retrace his fantasy to deal with the desire of the Other.

- The pervert can reaffirm his position as an object of enjoyment, intensifying his actions to manipulate the Other.

---

**Clinical Implications**

1. **In hysteria:**

- The clinical work involves helping the subject to recognize how the lack organizes his/her relationship with desire and the Other, allowing him/her to sustain his/her desire without depending exclusively on the recognition of others.

2. **In perversion:**

- The treatment aims to explore the denial of the lack and how the subject instrumentalizes the Other to sustain his/her position. The clinic seeks to open space for the recognition of one's own lack.

The tension between demand and enjoyment, exposed by the lack, is central to the path of each subject and can be analyzed in terms of his/her structural position and the paths he/she chooses to deal with the Other.

Love and sexual difference: doubled partners in Men and women

# The concept of **love and sexual difference** , particularly the idea of ​​\*\*doubled partners in men a...

The concept of **love and sexual difference** , particularly the idea of ​​**doubled partners in men and women** , is deeply rooted in psychoanalytic theory, as well as philosophical inquiries into relationships and desire. Below, we explore this theme through a Lacanian lens, integrating its implications for gender, desire, and subjectivity.

---

**1. Doubled Partners: A Psychoanalytic Perspective**
The notion of "doubled partners" refers to how men and women organize their relationships with love, desire, and sexual difference. According to psychoanalysis, this doubling can manifest in various forms:
**In Men:**

- **The Split Between Love and Desire:**
  Men often experience a split between the woman they love and the one they desire. Lacan describes this as the division between the **idealized Other** and the **object of jouissance** .
  - The partner in love may represent purity or idealized femininity.

  - The partner in desire embodies the realm of bodily satisfaction or transgression.

  - This split creates a duality in how men report to their partners, oscillating between admiration and objectification.

- **The Role of the Phallus:**
  In Lacanian theory, the phallus functions as a signifier of desire and power, shaping the man’s relationship with his partner(s). Men might pursue multiple partnerss to affirm their symbolic position or mask a perceived lack.
  **In Women:**
- **Identification and Rivalry:**
  Women may experience a doubling of partners through **identification** with another woman (often the mother) or through rivalry for the love of the same man. This dynamic plays out in the triangulation of desire.

- **The Question of "What Does He Want?"**
  Women, positioned as the **Other** in Lacan’s framework, are often concerned with deciphering the man’s desire. This interpretative position can lead to doubling:
  - One partner may represent stability or security.

  - Another may represent excitement, transgression, or danger.

- **The Feminine Jouissance:**
  Lacan introduces the idea of **feminine jouissance** , an excess or surplus that cannot be entirely captured by the phallic order. This jouissance may lead women to seek a partner who embodies something beyond the symbolic (e.g., spiritual or mystical connections).

---

**2. Love, Sexual Difference, and Fantasy**
Lacanian psychoanalysis emphasizes that love and sexual difference are mediated by fantasy, which structures how subjects relate to their "doubled" partners.

- **Fantasy as a Frame:**
  - Fantasy determines how the subject organizes their desire and how they choose their partners.

  - For men, fantasy often positions one partner as the object of jouissance and the other as a figure of respect or idealization.

  - For women, fantasy may revolve around being the desired object or identifying with a rival.

- **Imaginary, Symbolic, and Real Dynamics:**
  - The **Imaginary** : Doubling can involve projections and identifications with idealized or rivalrous images of the Other.

  - The **Symbolic** : Partners are mediated by cultural and linguistic structures, such as the roles of wife, lover, mother, or provider.

  - The **Real** : The encounter with the Real may destabilize this doubling, revealing the gaps or impossibilities in relationships.

---

**3. Contemporary Implications**
In modern contexts, the idea of doubled partners reflects broader social and cultural changes:

- **Fluidity in Relationships:**
  - Non-monogamous or polyamorous arrangements often literalize the doubling of partners, with individuals navigating multiple emotional and sexual connections.

- **Challenges of Sexual Difference:**
  - The traditional binary between men and women is increasingly questioned, leading to new forms of partnership that challenge psychoanalytic categories.

- **The Role of Technology:**
  - Online dating and virtual connections create additional layers of doubling, where partners exist both in physical and digital realms.

---

**4. Clinical Reflections**
For psychoanalysts, the doubling of partners provides insight into:

- **Unconscious Desires:**
  How the subject negotiates love and desire reveals their underlying fantasies and structures of enjoyment.

- **Repetition and Choice:**
  Patterns of choosing partners may reflect unresolved conflicts or the repetition of early relational dynamics (e.g., with parental figures).

- **The Ethical Dimension of Love:**
  Love, for Lacan, is an act that involves acknowledging the lack in oneself and the Other. The challenge lies in moving beyond the doubling of partners to a more integrated and ethical relation with the Other.

---

**Conclusion**
The concept of "doubled partners" illuminates the complexities of love and sexual difference in men and women. It highlights the tension between idealization and desire, stability and transgression, and the roles that fantasy and structure play in organizing relationships. From a Lacanian perspective, this doubling is not merely a personal phenomenon but a reflection of the fundamental structures of human subjectivity and the symbolic order. Understanding these dynamics allows for deeper engagement with the possibilities and limits of love.

Resume the text from Renata Salecl

# Renata Salecl, in her exploration of **love and sexual difference** , examines how societal norms an...

Renata Salecl, in her exploration of **love and sexual difference** , examines how societal norms and unconscious structures influence relationships between men and women. She delves into the **doubled partners** dynamic, where men often split their affection between a partner they love and one they desire, reflecting a division between idealization and jouissance. Women, on the other hand, navigate identification and rivalry, often positioning themselves in relation to what they perceive as the man's desire.Salecl situates these dynamics within Lacanian theory, highlighting how **fantasy, jouissance, and the symbolic order** mediate love and sexual difference. She also emphasizes the impact of modern societal shifts, such as changing gender roles and fluid relational structures, on traditional conceptions of love and desire.
Ultimately, Salecl's analysis underscores how relationships are shaped by the interplay between unconscious drives, cultural expectations, and personal fantasies, providing insights into the complexities of love in contemporary society.

Structure this: Why do men and women often double their partners into two fi gures, one a stable partner and the other an inaccessible lover? People prone to such doubling usually complain about the oppressiveness of their mar-riages and dream about escaping the constraints that their partners sup-posedly impose on them. But when their unhappy marriages actually come to an end, the complainers often lose the grounds of their existence and conf i ne themselves to a passionless, solitary life. Most of these cases present hysterical theaters: the suf f ering partner fi nds all the enjoyment precisely in suf f ering, thus a real change in the situation brings a catas-trophe for the hysteric. But if it is easy to understand the theater of the hysteric, the question still remains: what kind of roles do the two part-ners (for example, the oppressive husband and the unattainable lover) play in this drama? Do women and men redouble their partners in the same way? Specif i cally, how does sexual dif f erence af f ect such redou-bling? And how does the redoubling of the partners relate to the fact that the subject who tries to incite the love of another often presents himself or herself as someone else, that is, redoubles himself or herself, in order to attract the attention of the loved one?

# ### The Doubling of Partners: Men, Women, and Sexual Difference ...

### The Doubling of Partners: Men, Women, and Sexual Difference

**1. The Phenomenon of Doubling Partners**
Men and women often divide their partners into two distinct figures:

- A **stable, reliable partner** , representing security and commitment.

- An **inaccessible lover** , embodying desire and unattainability.

This dynamic emerges from dissatisfaction in relationships, where individuals feel trapped by the perceived constraints imposed by their stable partner. Despite dreaming of liberation from these oppressive bonds, the end of such relationships often leaves them unmoored, leading to solitude and a lack of passion.
**2. The Role of Hysteria**
In many cases, this doubling reveals a **hysterical theater** :

- The suffering partner derives **enjoyment precisely through their suffering** .

- When real change occurs, it results in a catastrophe, as the hysteric loses the structure that sustained their existence.
  **3. The Dramatic Roles of the Partners**
  This dynamic raises the question:
- What roles do the two partners play? For example, how do the **oppressive husband** and the **unattainable lover** serve as characters in the drama?

- Is the doubling of partners **gendered** , and do men and women engage in it differently?
  **4. Sexual Difference and Redoubling**
  Sexual difference profoundly shapes how men and women experience and enact this doubling:
- Women and men may redouble their partners differently, influenced by their relationship to **desireand jouissance** .

- Furthermore, the subject often **redoubles themselves** to incite the love of the other—presenting a version of themselves designed to attract attention.

This interplay of doubling both the partners and the self highlights the complex relationship between love, desire, and sexual difference, offering insight into the unconscious mechanisms that sustain romantic relationships.

Between writing and screaming

# The psychoanalytic analysis of the tension between the **written** and the **scream** can be thought of from different perspectives:1. **The Scream as the First Mark of the Subject**

The scream can be understood as a primordial, pre-linguistic manifestation, an appeal to the Other that inaugurates the relationship between the body and language. It is the subject's first gesture toward the Other, charged with urgency and not yet structured in the symbolic.

- **In the Real**: The scream is pure affect, an expression of an excess that cannot find words to translate it.

- **In the Symbolic**: The scream can be "tamed" by being converted into language, that is, into words, into an articulated discourse.

- **In the Imaginary**: The scream can be interpreted as a search for recognition, a request for the Other to see and respond to the subject.

2. **Written as Structuring**

Written, in turn, is the attempt to fix and order that which escapes in the scream. It marks an inscription in the symbolic, transforming what was ephemeral (sound, voice) into something permanent and transmissible.

- Written brings **distance and reflection**, while the scream is immediate and visceral.

- Lacan would say that written is closer to the letter and the dimension of enjoyment, while the scream belongs to the drive and desire.

3. **Between Written and Scream**

Between scream and written, the subject oscillates. This tension reflects:

- **The struggle between desire and lack**: the scream expresses the attempt to fill a lack, while writing is the attempt to circumscribe and name this lack.

- **Anguish and elaboration**: the scream can arise where words fail, while writing allows us to transform anguish into discourse.

4. **Psychoanalytic Clinic: From Scream to Writing**

In analysis, the work often involves transforming the scream into words, and eventually into a written form that can support the subject. This means:

- Making the subject move from the repetition of the scream (trauma) to the possibility of elaborating and narrating his/her experience.

- Recognizing that not everything can be written, and that the scream remains as a trace of the impossible to say, a residue of the real. 5. **Screaming and Writing in Culture**
  In contemporary times, screaming often appears as an expression of immediate demands, protests or discontent (on social media, for example), while writing resists as a space for reflection and archiving of desire.

Thus, the challenge for the subject is to find a balance between the scream that expresses the drive and the writing that allows for symbolization, building a bridge between the urgency of affection and the structure of language.

Ways of doing something:

I can make it happen, I can wait for the other to do it, I can let the world take care of it
Logic and structure

# The ways of doing something can be analyzed by the logic of action, waiting and delegation, based on the subject's position in relation to the Other and the world. From a psychoanalytic perspective, these options reveal the relationship between desire, enjoyment, responsibility and lack.

---

**1. I can make it happen**

- **Logic:** The subject assumes the active position, takes control of the situation and seeks to shape reality according to his/her desire.

- **Structure:**
- **Phallic**: There is a feeling of power and dominance, related to phallic enjoyment, which believes in the effectiveness of the act itself.

- **Imaginary**: It is based onbelief that the subject has the means or ability to control the outcome.

- **Symbolic**: Assumes responsibility for what he/she does, subscribing to the chain of desire and facing the consequences.

- **Clinical implications:**
- May lead to overactivity or compulsion to control, denying otherness or inherent lack.

- Risk of frustration when faced with the impossibility of doing everything alone.

--- **2. I can wait for the other to do it**

- **Logic:** The subject transfers responsibility to the Other (whether a specific person or a symbolic instance), trusting or demanding that the realization comes from outside.

- **Structure:**
- **Phallic and Symbolic**: Involves belief in the power or function of the Other as an agent of accomplishment.

- **Imaginary passivity**: Waiting can mask a dependence or alienation in relation to the desire of the Other.

- **Real**: The subject may experience anguish or disappointment if the Other does not meet his/her expectations.

- **Clinical implications:**
- Risk of resentment or constant complaints against the Other who "failed".

- In more serious cases, it may generate inertia or melancholy, in the refusal to assume one's own desiring position.

---

**3. I can let the world take care of itself**

- **Logical:** Here there is a delegation to contingency, chance or the course of the world itself. The subject abdicates a place of direct control.

- **Structure:**
- **Real**: Acceptance of lack and the impossible, allowing desire to operate in a less directed way.

- **Symbolic**: May represent trust in the order of the Other or in destiny.

- **Imaginary**: May also indicate an escape from responsibility, disguised as "letting go".

- **Clinical implications:**
- It can be liberating when it is not a refusal, but a commitment to opening up the possible.

- Risk of apathy or lack of engagement in life.

---

**Dynamics Between the Three Forms**

- These forms are not mutually exclusive and can alternate or coexist.

- **Balance:** A structured subject can navigate between these positions, assuming control when necessary, recognizing the role of the Other, and accepting the contingent without paralysis.

- **Imbalance:** Insisting exclusively on one of them can lead to rigidity, suffering, or difficulty in achieving.

---

**Conclusion**
These forms express how the subject deals with desire, enjoyment, and lack, placing themselves in different positions in their relationship with the Other and the world. In the clinic, the work would be to expand the subject's capacity to move between these positions, with responsibility and openness to the possible.

How these positions could be interpreted geometrically with the Klein bottle

# The Klein Bottle, with its characteristic of being a non-orientable and continuous surface, offers a rich geometric metaphor for interpreting the three positions described above. Each position — "make it happen", "wait for the other to do it" and "let the world take care of it" — can be located in different trajectories or movements within this topological structure.

---

**1. Making It Happen: the active position and the search for control**

- **Geometric interpretation:**
- This position can be represented by the path of a point that tries to trace and dominate a defined trajectory on the surface of the Klein Bottle, without realizing that this surface is paradoxically continuous and without a clear distinction between inside and outside.

- The "active" subject seeks control, but in the Bottle, this attempt to delimit a space (or an absolute enjoyment) always returns to the starting point, confronting the impossibility of totalizing desire.

- **Symbolism in the Bottle:**
- The search to make it happen would be like going through the bottleneck, believing that one is leaving the structure, but inevitably returning to it.

---

**2. Waiting for the Other to Do Itr: transfer of responsibility**

- **Geometric interpretation:**
- Here, the subject fixates on a point in the Klein Bottle, waiting for another moving point or even the structure's own path to solve the problem.

- This position is equivalent to "parking" in an illusory zone of inside or outside, without realizing that in the Klein Bottle these distinctions do not exist. The subject is intrinsically connected to the Other (to the structure), but refuses to recognize its implication.

- **Symbolism in the Bottle:**
- This point is at rest on the surface, but participates in the global movement without directly interfering. The illusion of separation between the subject and the Other is undone by the continuity of the Bottle.

---

**3. Letting the World Take Charge: surrender to the contingent**

- **Geometric interpretation:**
- This position can be visualized as a freer movement along the surface, accepting the topology of the Bottle without trying to dominate it or escape from it.

- The subject who "lets it flow" recognizes the paradoxical and contingent structure of the Bottle, accepting that every movement (or lack thereof) is inscribed in an inescapable continuity.

- **Symbolism in the Bottle:**
- This stance comes close to accepting lack and impossibility, allowing desire to articulate itself without illusions of closure.

---

**The Klein Bottle and the Dynamics Between the Three Forms**
The Klein Bottle represents the impossibility of rigid distinctions, and this reflects the dynamics between the three positions:

1. **Continuum between Activity and Passivity:**

- The attempt to make things happen and the wait for the Other are connected, like trajectories in the Bottle that inevitably meet. The active subject ends up facing the limits imposed by the Other, while the passive subject participates in the movement of the structure without recognizing it.

2. **Acceptance of Contingency:**

- "Letting the world take care of itself" corresponds to the recognition of the continuous topology of the Bottle, in which the subject gives up the illusion of total control or absolute separation.

3. **Crossing Fantasy:**

- In psychoanalysis, crossing fantasy can be metaphorically understood as crossing the Klein Bottle, recognizing that the paradoxical surface (desire and enjoyment) does not have an absolute "outside" and that the subject's choices are always inscribed in the structure.

---

**Conclusion**
The Klein Bottle, as a geometric metaphor, allows us to understand these positions as continuous movements within a complex and inescapable structure. The attempt to escape or control the movement returns to the subject; waiting for the Other reveals the illusion of separation; and surrendering to the flow represents the acceptance of contingency and lack, allowing a new articulation of desire.

The difficult position of begging for friends, demand, refusal, deprivation, resentment

# The position of begging for friends, in psychoanalytic and Lacanian terms, reveals a complex dynamic that involves **demand** , **refusal** , **deprivation** and **resentment** , each operating as an inscription in the field of desire, enjoyment and the relationship with the Other. This position, marked by a particular suffering, can be analyzed through the following axes:

---

**1. Begging as a Demand to the Other**

- **Demand:**
  Begging is, essentially, formulating a demand to the Other (the friend), exposing a lack. The act of asking is situated between the desire for recognition and the material or emotional need. When begging, the subject asks not only for something concrete, but also for the guarantee of his existence to the Other.

- **Tension:**
  The demand makes explicit the subject's dependence on the Other, exposing the structural lack. This can generate shame, as it is an exposure of vulnerability, but also resentment, due to the response that may fall short of expectations.

---

**2. Refusal and the Limits of the Other**

- **RefusalFriend's:**
  The refusal of a request, especially in close relationships, confronts the subject with the limits of the Other. The refusal can be experienced as a "no" to the subject himself, intensifying feelings of humiliation and rejection.

- **The Enjoyment of the Other:**
  The refusal can also be interpreted as a gesture of enjoyment of the Other, perceived as indifferent or cruel. The friend, by refusing, occupies a symbolic position of power, which can accentuate resentment.

---

**3. Deprivation: the Radical Lack**

- **Deprivation versus Castration:**
  Deprivation, unlike castration, implies a more radical lack, something that the subject feels as absolutely impossible to be filled. Begging puts the subject in contact with this dimension, to the extent that he realizes that the Other may not offer him what he wants, nor what he needs.

- **Helplessness:**
  The feeling of deprivation generates helplessness, since the subject finds himself excluded from the possibility of reciprocity or symbolic exchange. This exclusion can open space for the fantasy of total rejection.

---

**4. Rancor and the Enjoyment of Hate**

- **Rancor as Return:**
  Rancor arises as a reaction to the failure of the demand. It is a bitter enjoyment, where the subject fixates on the experience of rejection and feeds fantasies of revenge or resentment.

- **Hate as Defense:**
  Rancor can be a defense against humiliation. Instead of recognizing the lack as structural, the subject projects onto the rejecting friend the responsibility for his suffering. Thus, the friend becomes the hated object.

---

**5. The Difficult Dynamic of the Beggar and the Friend**

- **The Other as Ideal:**
  The friend, as Other, is often idealized as someone who should meet the subject's needs. This idealization creates fertile ground for resentment when expectations are not met.

- **Ambivalence:**
  The friend can simultaneously occupy the positions of savior and traitor, depending on his or her response to the demand. This ambivalence makes the relationship deeply conflictual.

---

**6. Way Out**

- **Elaboration of Desire:**
  Recognizing that the Other is not all-powerful and cannot meet all demands is a fundamental step. This implies accepting symbolic castration and lack as constitutive of the subject and the Other.

- **Redefinition of the Relationship:**
  Working on the relationship with the friend in a less idealized and more realistic way can reduce the intensity of feelings of humiliation and resentment.

- **Sublimation:**
  Sublimating the experience of deprivation can transform suffering into something productive, such as reflection, art or social bonding.

---

**Conclusion**

The position of begging for friends is not only a question of material need, but also a structural phenomenon that articulates lack, enjoyment and relationship with the Other. The response to this position requires the acceptance of the limit of the Other and the elaboration of a new relationship with desire and lack. The crossing of this position can open the way to more sustainable and less painful forms of relationship with oneself and with the Other.

Lacan identified with the clinical structure of Psychosis
The drive towards women as a symptom in Psychosis
Lacan would identify himself as a trans woman

# The question of Lacan identifying with the clinical structure of psychosis and with the drive towards women as a sym...

The question of Lacan identifying with the clinical structure of psychosis and with the drive towards women as a symptom involves delicate interpretations of his theories and his own life. Lacan never explicitly stated that he identified as psychotic or as a trans woman. However, we can explore these themes based on fundamental concepts of his work:

---

**1. Lacan and the Clinical Structure of Psychosis**

- **Foreclosure of the Name-of-the-Father:**
  Psychosis, in Lacan, is defined by the foreclosure of the signifier of the Name-of-the-Father, which prevents the symbolic stabilization of the subject and can lead to experiences such as delusions or hallucinations.
- There are moments in his seminars where Lacan seems to approach thepsychosis, especially when discussing his fascination with the logic of the signifier and the "pull to meaning" present in delusions.

- However, Lacan positions himself as a clinical observer and not as someone clinically psychotic.

- **Identification and Empathy:**
  Lacan, as a psychoanalyst, studied psychosis in depth, seeking to understand its structural mechanisms. This theoretical proximity can be confused with identification, but there is no direct evidence that Lacan claimed such a position.

---

**2. The Push to the Woman as a Symptom in Psychosis**

- **The "Push to the Woman":**
  In the seminar _"More, Still" (Encore)_, Lacan proposes that "woman does not exist" in the sense that there is no universal feminine position in the symbolic. Woman, as a position of jouissance, is always marked by incompleteness. - In the case of psychosis, the "pull towards woman" can manifest itself as an attempt to fill this void by identifying with the feminine position, whether through an imaginary or corporal construction.

- This phenomenon is often observed in psychotics who, in order to stabilize themselves, assume feminine characteristics or identities as a subjective solution.

- **Lacan and Femininity:**
  Lacan theorized about femininity as something accessible to all subjects, regardless of their biological sex. He himself recognized "feminine" aspects in his subjectivity, but this does not imply that he assumed or identified himself as a trans woman.

---

**3. Lacan as a Trans Woman?**

- **Femininity and the Position of Enjoyment:**
  Lacan explored femininity not as a question of gender identity, but as a position of enjoyment. Any subject, regardless of their clinical structure, can access "feminine enjoyment" (a enjoyment beyond the phallic).

- It is important to distinguish between feminine jouissance as a theoretical position and the experience of a feminine gender identity.

- **The Symbolic and Identity:**
  Lacan was a subversive thinker, but there is no concrete evidence that he lived or identified as a trans woman. This interpretation would be anachronistic and projected from contemporary categories that were not part of his time.

---

**4. Final Reflection**
Lacan, as an enigmatic and provocative figure, challenged established norms both in his work and in his personal stance. His approach to the drive towards women and psychosis reflects his theoretical-clinical interest more than a direct identification. He demystified the categories of gender and identity by emphasizing the function of the symbolic and jouissance, but this does not place him directly in the position of a trans woman. **Conclusion:**
Lacan identified with the position of explorer of the unconscious and clinical structures, but any inference about whether he identified as psychotic or as a trans woman seems more like an interpretative extrapolation than an evidence-based conclusion.

"Don't ask me for anything"
Analysis of people who can't stand lack

# The phrase "Don't ask me for anything" can be interpreted psychoanalytically as a defensive positioning...

The phrase "Don't ask me for anything" can be interpreted psychoanalytically as a defensive positioning in the face of lack, linked to the impossibility of fully satisfying the Other's demand. People who can't stand lack may adopt this stance as a way of avoiding confrontation with the Other's desire, their own incompleteness, and the impact of this on their subjectivity.

---

**1. Lack in Desire and Psychic Structure**

- **Lack as Structural:**
  For Lacan, lack (_manque_) is central to the constitution of the subject. It is represented by what is lost in the symbolic field, that is, that which can never be completely said or filled by the signifier.
- Those who "cannot bear the lack" try to deny or erase this essential structure, rejecting the desire or demand of the Other.

- **Desire and Demand:**
  Desire is always provoked by lack. By saying "don't ask me for anything", the subjectIto tries to avoid the position of being desired or responsible for the Other's desire. This may indicate a fear of not corresponding or being insufficient.

---

**2. Defense Mechanisms**

- **Refusal of the Symbolic Relationship:**
  "Don't ask me for anything" may be a defense mechanism against entering into a symbolic bond, which presupposes exchange, reciprocity and, inevitably, frustration.

- **Foraclusion of Lack:** By avoiding the request, the subject seeks to avoid recognizing the lack, both in the Other and in himself.

- **Narcissism:** The denial of lack may be related to an attempt to preserve an ideal image of oneself as self-sufficient and invulnerable.

- **Escape from the Field of Desire:**
  By refusing demands, the subject may be trying to avoid situations in which he feels "put at fault" or incapable of fully meeting the Other's needs. This stance can be observed in clinical structures such as psychosis, where there are difficulties in dealing with symbolic castration.

---

**3. Subjective and Relational Position**

- **Loneliness and Self-Sufficiency:**
  Those who refuse requests often seek to protect themselves from bonds that may expose their vulnerability or their inability to respond to the Other. This stance can lead to isolation.

- **Demand Anguish:**
  The Other's demand can be perceived as invasive or threatening, as it evokes the subject's position as lacking, in debt or in relationship.

---

**4. Clinical Implications**

- **Clinic of Foreclosure and Refusal:**
  Subjects who cannot bear the lack often present rigid defenses against the Other's desire. In the clinic, this can be addressed by working on tolerance to frustration and acceptance of incompleteness as constitutive of the human being.

- **Recognition of the Other:**
  The challenge is to help the subject recognize that the desire of the Other is not necessarily a threat, but an opportunity for the construction of new meanings.

---

**5. Final Considerations**
The phrase "Don't ask me for anything" translates a gesture of refusal in the face of otherness, desire and lack itself. Psychoanalysis recognizes lack as inescapable, and its acceptance can open space for more authentic bonds that are less marked by anguish or isolation. Working on these dynamics requires that the subject learn to deal with the limits of his or her condition, recognizing himself or herself as lacking, but not for that reason less capable of desiring or being in a relationship.

Those who are not my providers hate me
Those who do not attribute knowledge to me hate them
Those who do not undergo analysis with me
Those who do not work for me

# These statements can be analyzed as reflections on relationships of dependence, authority, knowledge and transference, highlighting dynamics of identification, rivalry and rejection in the field of desire and the bond with the Other.

---

**1. Providers and hate** **"Those who are not my providers hate me":**

- **Relationship with the phallus/symbolic provision:**
  The idea of ​​"provider" is linked to the figure of the one who supplies the lack. In psychoanalysis, the provider can be understood as someone who symbolically occupies the phallic position, capable of offering something that the subject desires or needs.
- The refusal or absence of this provision can be experienced as a threat or abandonment, generating hate as a defensive response.

- **Dependence and autonomy:**
  Those who do not depend may also refuse to occupy the position of sustainer of another's desire, which may be experienced by the subject as rejection or indifference, generating resentment or rancor.

---

**2. Knowledge and hate** **"Those who do not impute knowledge to me hate them":**

- **Knowledge and transference:**
  In analytic transference, the subject often imputes knowledge to the analyst, positioning him as the one who "knows about his desire". Those who do not recognize this position of knowledge may be seen as disinterested or hostile.
- The absence of imputationThe act of knowing can be interpreted as a refusal to take the symbolic place of authority, leading the subject to feel disqualified or rejected.

- **Hatred of knowledge:**
  On the other hand, knowledge itself can be a source of hatred, especially when it evokes castration or reveals a lack in the subject and in the Other. Knowledge, in this sense, is not neutral; it destabilizes, causes anguish and can generate resistance.

---

**3. Transference and absence** **"Those who do not undergo analysis with me":**

- **Analytical relationship and exclusivity:**
  The analytical bond is marked by transference, which often becomes intense and exclusive. The absence of analysis with a subject can be experienced as a refusal or lack of recognition, generating feelings of exclusion or projected hatred.

---**The lack of the Other:**
Those who do not enter into the transference relationship can be seen as someone who refuses to get involved in the subject's desire, exposing the loneliness of the lack in their own desire.

----**4. Work and recognition** **"Those who don't work for me":**

- **Power and submission:**
  Working for someone implies recognition, submission to a field of power and, often, symbolic validation. Those who do not occupy this position may be perceived as someone who challenges or devalues ​​the subject's authority, which may arouse feelings of hatred or rejection.

- **Relationship with the Other:**
  The lack of a work relationship may be experienced as an absence of exchange or reciprocity, which exposes the subject to the perception of isolation and helplessness.

---

**5. Psychoanalytic implications**

- **Hatred and negative transference:**
  These dynamics point to manifestations of negative transference, where hatred arises in relation to the Other who does not occupy a desired position (provides, recognizes knowledge, works).

- **Identification and rejection:**
  The lack of identification with the Other can be experienced as a threat to subjective stability, generating defense mechanisms such as hatred, resentment or negative idealization.

- **Lack at the center of the social bond:**
  Lacan emphasizes that every relationship is mediated by lack. Hatred, in these cases, can be seen as an attempt to deny or deal with one's own castration, projecting it onto the Other who "does not occupy the ideal place".

---

**Conclusion**
These sentences highlight how much human relationships are permeated by issues of lack, power and recognition. Psychoanalysis allows us to address these tensions, helping the subject to recognize the role of desire and lack in their relationships, transforming hatred into a path for subjective work and new forms of bonding with the Other.

Treatment direction and the principles of its power, Lacanian Psychoanalysis

# Treatment direction and the principles of its power in Lacanian psychoanalysis are fundamental concepts, which involve the ethics of psychoanalysis, the position of the analyst, the structure of desire and the subjectivation of the analysand. Below is an analysis based on Lacan's teachings:

---

**1. Treatment Direction**
The direction of treatment refers to the guidance that the analyst gives to the analysand's analytic path, considering the particularities of his subjective structure (neurosis, psychosis, perversion) and his mode of relating to desire, jouissance and the Other.
**Main points:**

- **The analyst's desire:**
  The analyst does not impose his own desires, but positions himself as an empty place that allows the subject to confront his desire. The analyst's desire is not a conscious intention, but an ethical position that aims to support symbolic castration and the work of the unconscious.

- **The logic of the unconscious:**
  The direction of the treatment respects the principle that the unconscious is structured like a language. The analyst acts based on the formations of the unconscious (lapses, dreams, failed acts), leading the analysand to decipher its messages.

- **The crossing of fantasy:**
  TheAnalysis seeks to allow the subject to traverse his fundamental fantasy, dismantling the imaginary structures that sustain his relationship with enjoyment and desire. Fantasy is a support for desire, but also a limit that prevents subjective change.

---

**2. Principles of His Power**
The power of the analyst in directing the treatment does not reside in imposition or suggestion, but in the way he handles the transference, the signifier and the void that constitutes the place of the subject.
**Fundamental ethical principles:**

- **The destitution of the analyst's personal power:**
  Lacan emphasizes that the analyst must operate "without wanting to be a master". The analyst's power lies in the symbolic function he occupies, not in his ego or personal authority.

- **Transference as the driving force of treatment:**
  The analyst works with transference, which is the love addressed to the knowledge supposedly held by the analyst. This knowledge is not real, but supposed by the analysand, allowing the analytic work to occur.

- **The handling of the object a:**
  The analyst positions himself as an "object a" in certain phases of the analysis, that is, as the cause of desire, to destabilize imaginary identifications and open space for the emergence of the analysand's desire.

- **Symbolic castration:**
  The analyst, by sustaining symbolic castration, leads the analysand to assume his own lack-of-being, separating himself from the ideal of completeness.

**The question of power:**

- The analyst does not exercise power over the analysand, but rather enables the subject to discover his own power to signify and take responsibility for his desire.

- Lacan distinguishes the analyst's power from any form of suggestion or domination. Power resides in listening and operating with the symbolic structure.

---

**3. Ethical Direction**
Lacanian psychoanalysis differs from normative therapeutic practices by following an ethical direction, which is based on the singularity of the subject and the truth of desire.

---

**4. Ethical Direction**
Lacanian psychoanalysis differs from normative therapeutic practices by following an ethical direction, which is based on the singularity of the subject and the truth of desire. **The pillars of Lacanian ethics:**

- **Not giving in to desire:**
  Analysis leads the subject to not give up on his fundamental desire, facing the impasses of his structure without seeking normative or adaptive solutions.

- **Truth in discourse:**
  Analysis is a work of deciphering the unconscious, where the subject is confronted with the partial truths that emerge in discourse.

- **Lack as a structuring factor:**
  Psychoanalysis recognizes that the subject is always at fault, and this lack is constitutive of his subjectivity and his desire.

---

**4. Clinical Implications**

- **In neurosis:**
  The treatment aims to lead the subject to question his symptomatic formations and the structure of his fantasy, enabling new ways of relating to desire.

- **In psychosis:**
  The work consists of constructing a symbolic anchoring point, using resources such as stabilization by letter or by naming.

- **In perversion:**
  The analyst seeks to displace the subject from his position as master of enjoyment, allowing a questioning of the relationship with the Other.

---

**5. Conclusion**
The direction of treatment in Lacanian psychoanalysis is guided by the ethics of desire and respect for the singularity of the subject. The principles of its power reside in the symbolic function of the analyst, who does not govern the analysand, but accompanies him on the path of assuming his lack and his desire. This approach challenges any notion of normalization, promoting a radical subjectivation that transforms the subject's relationships with the Other, with enjoyment and with knowledge.

Clinical implications for each of the structures as a criterion for ending analysis: More-than-enjoyment, social bond

# In Lacanian psychoanalysis, the clinical implications for the criterion for ending analysis vary according to each clinical structure (neurosis, psychosis and perversion) and are directly related to the way the subject deals with **more-than-enjoyment** and the **social bond**. Below, we will explainThese dimensions are lora for each structure.

---

**1. Neurosis**

Neurosis (hysteria or obsessive neurosis) is marked by the conflict with desire and the presence of fantasy as a support for the subject's relationship with enjoyment and the Other.
**Termination criteria:**

1. **More-than-enjoyment:**

- The subject must recognize more-than-enjoyment as that which exceeds phallic enjoyment and sustains the symptom. The termination occurs when the subject manages to situate enjoyment in a position that does not enslave him, but allows him to deal with it in a less symptomatic way.

- The crossing of fantasy is central: the subject ceases to be a prisoner of the fantasy that organizes his enjoyment and finds new forms of subjectivation.

2. **Social bond:**

- The analysand must be able to reconfigure his relationship with the Other. In the case of hysteria, this may mean leaving the position of object of desire of the Other. For the obsessive, it implies breaking with the narcissistic confinement and the refusal to get involved in the social bond.

- The social bond must be reestablished without depending exclusively on the recognition of the Other to sustain desire.

---

**2. Psychosis**

In psychosis, the absence of the paternal metaphor results in a direct relationship with enjoyment, without the symbolic mediation of the Name-of-the-Father. The treatment seeks to stabilize the subject, often by constructing a substitute.

**Termination criteria:**

1. **More-than-enjoyment:**

- The objective is not to extinguish enjoyment, but to organize it through a naming or a symbolic construction that allows the subject to deal with the devastating effects of enjoyment.

- Stabilization can occur through the construction of a sinthome that functions as an anchor point for enjoyment (such as writing, the use of art, or even a singular relationship with language).

2. **Social bond:**

- For the psychotic, the social bond can be a challenge due to the invasive presence of the Other. The termination occurs when the subject finds a unique way of relating to the Other without dissolving into it or completely alienating himself.

- This may include the creation of his own social circuits or stabilization in a specific field where the subject recognizes himself.

---

**3. Perversion**
In perversion, enjoyment is organized around the attempt to sustain the enjoyment of the Other, functioning as the master of enjoyment and operating with the transgression of symbolic laws.
**Termination criteria:**

1. **More-than-enjoyment:**

- The termination implies displacing the subject from the position of agent of the Other's enjoyment, allowing him to recognize the limit of his relationship with enjoyment.

- The pervert must be led to confront symbolic castration and question the omnipotence of his knowledge about enjoyment.

2. **Social bond:**

- The analytical work aims to enable the perverted subject to build a social bond that does not depend exclusively on his/her function as an instrument of the Other's enjoyment.
- An effective end is reached when the subject can recognize the Other as incomplete and position himself/herself in a less parasitic manner in relation to the social bond.

---

| **General Comparison of Criteria** | Structure                                                           | More-than-enjoyment                                            | Social Bond |
