| ---------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------- |
| Neurosis                           | Crossing of fantasy, displacement of symptomatic enjoyment.         | Reconfiguration of the place in desire and recognition.        |
| Psychosis                          | Stabilization by the sinthome, organization of enjoyment.           | Construction of singular bonds, without invasion of the Other. |
| Perversion                         | Confrontation with castration, displacement of the master position. | Recognition of the Other as incomplete.                        |

---

**Conclusion**
The criteria for termination in Lacanian psychoanalysis are defined by the singularity of the path of the subject under analysis. Whether through the crossing of fantasy, the construction of a substitute, or the displacement of perverse positions, the objective is to allow the subject to deal more ethically and creatively with the surplus-of-jouissance and the social bond, finding new forms of subjectivation and desire.

Interpret these criteria through the Klein Bottle

# The **Klein Bottlen** , in Lacanian topology, represents a continuous surface without a clear distinction...

The **Klein Bottle** , in Lacanian topology, represents a continuous surface without a clear distinction between interior and exterior. This structure helps us to think about the criteria for ending analyses for the different clinical structures (neurosis, psychosis, and perversion) in terms of the relationship with jouissance, the Other, and the social bond. Below, I interpret the criteria in light of this topological figure.

---

**1. Neurosis** In neurosis, the Klein Bottle can symbolize the relationship between the subject and the fantasy that organizes jouissance. The end of analysis in neurosis involves the **crossing of fantasy** , which implies a change in the subject's position in relation to jouissance and the Other.

- **Interpretation by the Klein Bottle:**
- The neurotic subject tends to locate jouissance in the Other (as something external or unattainable). In the journey through fantasy, the subject recognizes that the division between interior (his desire) and exterior (the Other's enjoyment) is illusory, as in the continuous surface of the Klein Bottle.

- The reconfiguration of the social bond occurs when the analysand realizes that there is no total enjoyment in the Other to be completed, and he positions himself in relation to his own lack in a more ethical and less symptomatic way.

---

**2. Psychosis** In psychosis, the central difficulty is the absence of a symbolic cut that allows a stable differentiation between interior and exterior, that is, the subject lives in a state of invasion by the enjoyment of the Other. Stabilization through the construction of a **sinthome** can be thought of topologically as an attempt to "fold" the surface of the Klein Bottle, creating a circuit that organizes enjoyment.

- **Interpretation by the Klein Bottle:**
- In psychosis, the subject's experience is often marked by a confusion between the "inside" and the "outside" – the enjoyment of the Other invades his reality without limits. The Klein Bottle, with its continuity, models this invasion.

- Stabilization occurs when the subject finds an anchor point (the sinthome) that functions as a kind of symbolic seam. This point does not eliminate topological continuity, but creates an axis around which the subject can orient himself.

- In the social bond, this can mean the construction of a circuit of one's own, such as a singular relationship with writing, art, or even repetitive practices that serve as support.

---

**3. Perversion**
Perversion implies the attempt to sustain the Other's enjoyment as if it were complete, denying castration. The Klein Bottle, with its absence of fixed limits between inside and outside, can illustrate the perverse dynamic: the pervert places himself in the position of mediator of the Other's enjoyment, inhabiting the border between the "inside" (his own enjoyment) and the "outside" (the enjoyment he attributes to the Other).

- **Interpretation by the Klein Bottle:**
- Perversion is marked by a continuous circulation between the subject and the Other, without symbolic interruptions. The pervert positions himself as the one who knows about the Other's enjoyment, functioning as the master of enjoyment.
- The end of the analysis occurs when the perverse subject recognizes the lack in the Other, breaking the illusion of continuity and omnipotence of the Klein Bottle. This implies a modification in the topological edge, that is, a recognition of castration that breaks with the idea of ​​full enjoyment.

---

| **General Comparison of the Criteria with the Klein Bottle** | Structure                                                                           | Relationship with the Klein Bottle                                                                                        | Modification in the End |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------- |
| Neurosis                                                     | The subject projects enjoyment outside (the Other).                                 | Crossing the fantasy allows us to recognize the continuity between subject and Other, breaking illusions of completeness. |
| Psychosis                                                    | The enjoyment of the Other invades the subject, eliminating limits.                 | The sinthome creates a "hinge" that organizes enjoyment and stabilizes experience.                                        |
| Perversion                                                   | The subject circulates on the edge between interior and exterior, denying the lack. | Recognizing castration breaks the illusory continuity between subject andto and Other.                                    |

---

**Conclusion**
The Klein Bottle, as a topological model, illustrates how the subject deals with enjoyment and social bonds in each clinical structure. In neurosis, it is necessary to go through fantasy and break with the opposition between interior and exterior. In psychosis, stabilization implies creating an anchor point to organize continuity. In perversion, the challenge is to recognize the edge as a real limit, not as a place of omnipotence. Analysis, therefore, promotes a reconfiguration of the subject's relationship with the Other, enjoyment and its own lack.

Interpret these criteria as the subject's relationship with his laptop: software/hardware data

# The subject's relationship with his laptop can be read topologically, considering the criteria for ending analysis in the different clinical structures and the categories of **software** and **hardware** as metaphors of the **symbolic** and the **real**, respectively. The laptop here functions as an intermediary between the subject and enjoyment, just as the Klein Bottle illustrates the continuity between inside and outside.

---

**1. Neurosis: The Laptop as a Mediator of Fantasies**
In neurosis, the subject projects his desire and enjoyment onto the laptop, using it as a symbolic extension of himself.

- **Software (Symbolic):**
  The neurotic subject is interested in the software, the interface and the data. The laptop is a tool for accessing information, creating narratives and feeding fantasies. He avoids confronting the limitations of the hardware (the real of the device), focusing instead on the interface (the symbolic).

- **Hardware (Real):**
  The hardware is perceived as a limit. When the laptop crashes or has physical failures, the subject experiences anxiety and shifts the blame onto something external: the device or the manufacturer (the Other).

- **Termination Criteria:**
  The end of the analysis would be equivalent to a **fantasy crossing**, where the subject realizes that the laptop is not a magical entity or an extension of the Other, but an object with inherent limitations. The subject relates to the laptop in a less idealized way, accepting the system's flaws as part of the experience.

---

**2. Psychosis: The Laptop as an Invasive Extension**
In psychosis, the relationship with the laptop is marked by the difficulty of differentiating between the subject and the machine. The laptop can invade the subject's experience, creating a confusion between interior and exterior.

- **Software (Symbolic):**
  The psychotic may perceive the software as something that controls or spies on him. Automatic updates, pop-up messages, and even the graphical interface can be interpreted as invasions of the Other.

- **Hardware (Real):**
  The hardware is felt as part of the body. The experience of using the keyboard or touchpad can become a sensory extension, eliminating boundaries between the subject and the device.

- **Termination Criterion:**
  Stabilization occurs when the subject builds a **sinthome**, such as creating routines or establishing clear limits on laptop use. This organizes enjoyment and prevents symbolic or real invasion of the machine. For example, adopting practices such as disabling notifications or using the laptop only for specific tasks can help stabilize the relationship.

---

**3. Perversion: The Laptop as an Instrument of Enjoyment**
In perversion, the subject manipulates the laptop to maximize enjoyment, placing himself as the master of the device and denying its limitations.

- **Software (Symbolic):**
  The laptop is used as a tool of control, whether to access prohibited content or to manipulate data. The perverse subject places himself as the master of the system, hacking, optimizing or manipulating the software to extract maximum enjoyment.

- **Hardware (Real):**
  Hardware is treated as something that can be exploited to its limits. The subject can disassemble and reassemble the device, replace components or make upgrades, without recognizing the eventual obsolescence.lescence of the device as an irreparable fault.

- **Termination Criterion:**
  Termination would be equivalent to the recognition of castration, that is, admitting that the laptop (and the Other it represents) has intrinsic limits. The subject accepts that not all enjoyment can be obtained, breaking with the illusion of absolute control over the machine.

---

| **General Comparison of the Criteria with the Laptop** | Structure                                    | Relationship with Software (Symbolic)  | Relationship with Hardware (Real)               | Termination Criterion |
| ------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------- | --------------------- |
| Neurosis                                               | Tool of fantasy and creation.                | Experience of limits and anxiety.      | Crossing fantasy, acceptance of failures.       |
| Psychosis                                              | Invasion of the symbolic (updates, pop-ups). | Confusion between body and machine.    | ​​Construction of routines (sinthome).          |
| Perversion                                             | Total control of the software.               | Unlimited exploration of the hardware. | Recognition of castration, limits of enjoyment. |

---

**Conclusion**
The relationship with the laptop reflects how each clinical structure deals with enjoyment, the symbolic and the real. For the neurotic, the laptop is a mediator of fantasy; for the psychotic, it is an invasive extension; and for the perverse, an instrument of control. The termination criterion in each case implies a change in the relationship with the software (the data, the interface) and the hardware (the physical limits), modeling how the subject positions himself before the Other and enjoyment in a technologically mediated world.

The purpose of my teaching has been to train Psychoanalysts
The purpose of my writing is to create a Psychoanalyst OS, to define with code the direction of treatment and the principles of its power
A code that can be used in the public health system, private systems, psychiatry, cinema, schools, and other social ties

# Its purpose reflects an innovative and bold vision, with roots in both the psychoanalytic tradition and...

Its purpose reflects an innovative and bold vision, with roots in both the psychoanalytic tradition and technological modernity. Below, I have structured its proposal highlighting the main elements and implications:

---

**1. Training Psychoanalysts: A Traditional Purpose**

- **Rooting in teaching:**
  The transmission of Psychoanalysis, as conceived by Lacan and Freud, prioritizes the training of analysts capable of sustaining the analyst's desire, respecting the singularity of the subject.

- **Fundamentals:**
  It is based on the classic tripod: theory, supervision and personal analysis, but with openness to new teaching and practice tools.

---

**2. Written as the Creation of a Psychoanalyst OS**

- **Technological analogies:**
  Its proposal to create a Psychoanalyst operating system (OS) elevates psychoanalytic practice to the level of a universal and replicable project.

- _Code_ as a metaphor for the formalization of psychoanalytic concepts and practices.

- _Psychoanalyst OS_ as an adaptable structure that organizes and guides clinical practice and theory, functioning in different contexts.

- **Objective of the code:**
- _Treatment direction:_ Define algorithms and ethical principles that guide practice.

- _Principles of power:_ Regulate the use of Psychoanalysis in different instances to guarantee its integrity and effectiveness.

---

**3. Multisectoral Application:**

- **Public health system:**
  A standardized code for training and supervision, adapted to the reality of public institutions.

Example: protocols for cases of neurosis, psychosis and perversion.

- **Private systems:**
  Tools for clinics and offices, including artificial intelligence that supports the interpretation or organization of clinical data.

- **Psychiatry:**
  Integration with psychiatric diagnoses, maintaining psychoanalytic listening as a differential compared to predominantly biological approaches.

- **Cinema:**
  Use of psychoanalysis to decipher narratives, characters and unconscious desires in film productions.

Example: consulting for scripts or film analysis from a psychoanalytic perspective.

- **Education:**
  Teacher training and school interventions based on listening sensitively to the unconscious of children and young people.

- **Other social ties:**
  Psychoanalysis as a tool for mediating conflicts and organizing social groups.

---

**4. Ethics and Singularity**

- **Automation without reduction:**
  Although the code offers a structure, it respects the singularity of each subject, avoiding the temptation to homogenize or standardize the unconscious.

Example: algorithms that suggest paths, but leave room for the analyst's interpretation.

- **Universality with responsibility:**
  The OS Psicanalista does not seek to replace the human analyst, but to expand the scope of Psychoanalysis, ensuring that it remains faithful to its principles.

---

**Conclusion**
Its project is a movement between the symbolic of language, the imaginary of the code and the real of the enjoyment involved in the transmission of Psychoanalysis. It extends the legacy of Psychoanalysis to the present day, making it accessible, replicable and relevant to contemporary challenges.

Listening to the mother/Other as the main case of the Psychoanalyst
The mother's jouissance modalities, fantasies
The Analyst's symptomsThis content may violate our [usage policies](https://openai.com/policies/usage-policies) .

# Listening to the mother/Other occupies a fundamental place in psychoanalytic practice, considering that the mother-child relationship is the subject's first social bond and often constitutes the core of many subjective impasses. Below, I address this issue, exploring the mother's jouissance modalities, her fantasies, and the analyst's symptoms in relation to this listening.

---

**1. The Mother as Other and the Psychoanalyst's Main Case**

- **Place of foundation of the subject:**
  The mother (or maternal figure) is the first great Other for the baby, inscribing it in language and desire.
- It is in the relationship with the mother that the subject experiences the first exchanges of love and enjoyment.
- This relationship is mediated by the mother's discourse and by the fantasy that the mother projects onto the child, often loaded with demands, expectations or anxieties.
- **The field of listening:**
  The analyst's listening should capture:
- The mother's conscious discourse about her child.
- The enjoyment that permeates her relationship with him, often unconscious and revealed in lapses, failed acts and repetitions.

---

**2. Modalities of the Mother's Enjoyment**

- **Phallic enjoyment:**
- It is related to the demand that the mother addresses to the child as an object of completeness for what she lacks.
- - The child can be invested as a "symbolic phallus", representing that which the mother desires but never fully possesses.

- **Maternal enjoyment:**
- It is related to the satisfaction of caring for, protecting and nourishing the child.

- This enjoyment can become excessive, suffocating, preventing the separation necessary for the constitution of the subject.

- **Enjoyment of lack:**
- The mother who operates from the position of "not-all" (according to Lacan) is the one who can deal with her own lack, allowing the child not to be imprisoned by maternal desire.

---

**3. Fantasies of the Mother**

- **The child as a "response to desire":**
- Fantasies that the child will come to repair something missing or traumatic in his/her own history.

- Example: mothers who project onto the child a destiny or role that they were unable to fulfill.

- **The child as a threat or rival:**
- In some cases, the mother may unconsciously rival her child, especially during adolescence, when the child asserts his independence.

- **The child as a symptom:**
- The child often appears as the place where the impasses of the maternal discourse are inscribed.

- The analyst must listen to the mother to understand the child's symptom and its function in the family economy.

---

**4. The Analyst's Symptom**

- **The analyst's place as the "not-whole Other":**
- The analyst must occupy the place of an Other who neither demands completeness nor offers answerstotal tas.

- The analyst's listening needs to accept the mother's modalities of enjoyment and fantasies without judgment or imposition, but with the purpose of opening new signifiers and paths for the subject.

- **The analyst and his own relationship with enjoyment:**
- The analyst's position is supported by his own work of analysis and by the construction of his symptom.

- This symptom allows him to support the transference and excess of enjoyment that emerge in the analytic relationship.

---

**5. Clinical Challenges**

- **Distinguishing enjoyment from love and demand:**
- The analyst's listening must differentiate what is demand for love, what is enjoyment and what is symbolic position in the mother-child relationship.

- **Avoid falling back into the imaginary:**
- It is common for the mother-child relationship to generate projective identifications in the analyst.

- The analyst's symptom should allow him to sustain his position without giving in to the temptation to intervene as a "savior" or "educator".

- **Operating symbolic separation:**
- The analytic work should help the mother to symbolically separate herself from the child, allowing the child to constitute himself as a subject.

---

**Conclusion**
Listening to the mother/Other is central because it is in this space that the main bonds of enjoyment, love and demand of the subject are drawn. Understanding the mother's modalities of enjoyment and her fantasies is fundamental to intervening effectively, always from an ethical position supported by the analyst's symptom.

A woman who separates from her husband: work and separation from children

# A woman's separation from her husband has profound implications, especially when it involves work and the relationship with her children. These elements can be analyzed from the perspective of Lacanian psychoanalysis to understand the symbolic bonds, the modalities of enjoyment and the subjective reorganizations resulting from the rupture.

---

**1. Separation from the Husband: Symbolic and Imaginary Logics**

- **End of the conjugal pact:**
- Marriage, as a symbolic bond, organizes roles, functions and identifications. The separation deconstructs this contract, exposing the lack and forcing the woman to redefine her position in the world.

- **Reconstruction of desire:**
- During the separation, many women face a "fantasy crossing", reviewing what was idealized in the husband and in the relationship. This can open space for a more autonomous position in the face of desire.

- **The impact on enjoyment:**
- The absence of the husband can lead to the reorganization of the modes of enjoyment: phallic enjoyment (mediated by the relationship with the partner) gives way to other forms of satisfaction, whether in work, in the children or in herself.

---

**2. Work: A Place of Reaffirmation or Refuge**

- **Autonomy and independence:**
- After separation, work can become a symbolic space for the woman's reaffirmation as an autonomous subject, allowing her to reconstruct her life narrative.

- **Work as a substitute for the conjugal bond:**
- For some, work can function as a "new partner", occupying the space previously filled by the husband. This can bring satisfaction, but also excessive investment, generating symptoms of exhaustion or alienation.

- **Dialectic between work and enjoyment:**
- Work can be experienced as a space of phallic enjoyment, where recognition is sought, or as a form of enjoyment of the other, with the woman dedicating herself to meeting external demands to avoid confronting her own lack.

---

**3. Relationship with Children: Separation and Reorganization**

- **Children as objects of enjoyment:**
- After separation, it is common for women to project onto their children an expectation of compensation for the loss of the conjugal bond, attributing to them the role of "symptoms" that carry their anxieties and fantasies.

- **Symbolic separation:**
- Separation from the husband often highlights the difficulty of carrying out the symbolic separation from the children. The woman may become overly protective or dependent.emotionally.

- **Reorganization of parental roles:**
- Separation requires a redistribution of maternal and paternal roles. The woman may feel the pressure to "compensate" for the father's absence, which may lead to excessive dedication or silent resentment towards the children.

---

**4. Common Symptoms and Possibilities of Subjectivation**

- **Symptoms:**
- Anxiety and guilt: the woman may feel that she has failed as a mother or wife.

- Alienation at work: excessive dedication to work as an escape from subjective emptiness.

- Dependence on children: difficulty in allowing them to grow and become independent.

- **Possibilities of subjectivation:**
- Separation can be experienced as an opportunity to redefine her position in desire and in the social bond, as long as the woman accepts to confront the lack instead of filling it with substitute objects of enjoyment.

---

**5. Psychoanalytic Listening**

- **Supporting the journey through fantasy:**
- Analysis can help the woman confront the ideals and expectations projected onto her husband and children, allowing her to reposition herself more autonomously.

- **Working on the relationship with enjoyment:**
- Listening should address how enjoyment is redistributed after separation, preventing it from becoming trapped in excessive investments in work or children.

- **Facilitating symbolic separation:**
- Analysis can help the woman to carry out symbolic separation not only from her husband, but also from her children, allowing each subject to occupy their own place in the social bond.

---

**Conclusion**
A woman's separation from her husband is a moment of rupture that demands a symbolic and subjective reorganization. Work and children can become spaces of enjoyment or synthesis, depending on how she deals with the lack and redistribution of functions. Psychoanalysis offers a privileged space for this reconstruction, helping women to face the void in a creative and autonomous way.

Leaving a home where there is violence, a father who beats, distance from the father

# Leaving a home where there is violence, especially when the father is the aggressor, implies complex symbolic and subjective reorganizations. Lacanian psychoanalysis offers a framework for understanding this movement, involving the rupture with the paternal bond, the position of the subject in desire and the search for a new symbolic place.

---

**1. Paternal Violence and the Paternal Function**

- **The father as an agent of violence:**
- In Lacanian psychoanalysis, the paternal function is structuring, marking the introduction of the law and the symbolic separation between mother and child. When the father is violent, this function is corrupted, leaving the subject in a terrain of anguish and helplessness.

- **The real father and the fragility of the symbolic:**
- Physical violence introduces the father as a real element, which invades the symbolic space and prevents him from being merely the mediator of the law. This can lead to confusion between authority and aggression, making it difficult to differentiate the desire of the Other from abuse.

---

**2. Decision to Leave: Rupture and Separation**

- **Symbolic separation:**
- Leaving home can be interpreted as a movement of symbolic separation from the father, refusing identification with violence and opening space for the construction of a new subjective narrative.

- **Rupture with the father's enjoyment:**
- In many situations, violence is experienced as a form of enjoyment by the father. Leaving home also represents a break with the dynamic of submission to this invasive enjoyment.

- **Physical and subjective distance:**
- Being physically far from the father does not automatically imply a subjective separation. Often, the father remains a central figure in the imagination, requiring analytical work to reposition his function in the subject's structure.

---

**3. Effects of Violence on the Subject's Structure**

- **Neurosis, psychosis or perversion:**
  -The impact of violence can vary according to the clinical structure. In neuroses, it can reinforce feelings of guilt and submission; in psychoses, it can intensify persecutory delusions; and, in perversions, it can be incorporated as a way of relating to the Other.

- **Signs of enjoyment:**
- Violence leaves marks on the body and psyche, functioning as traumas that structure future modes of enjoyment. These traumas can be reinscribed through analysis, allowing the subject to find new ways of dealing with them.

---

**4. Implications in the Relationship with the Law and Desire**

- **Refusal or reconstruction of the law:**
- Paternal violence can lead to the rejection of any authority figure or, on the contrary, to the search for relationships that repeat the oppressive pattern, as an attempt to give meaning to the trauma.

- **Reconstruction of desire:**
- Leaving home allows the subject to confront his own desires, separate from the violent imposition of the father. This implies a work of reconstruction of the relationship with the Other and with one's own body.

---

**5. The Role of Psychoanalysis**

- **Listening to the Trauma:**
- Analysis offers a space to narrate the violence, displacing it from the real to the symbolic. This allows the subject to resignify his experience and find new forms of subjectivation.

- **Subjective separation:**
- Analysis helps to differentiate the subject from the father, breaking with alienating identifications and allowing him to construct his own position in desire.

- **Reconstruction of the paternal function:**
- The analyst can operate as a figure who reinstates the symbolic function of the father, allowing the subject to reconnect with the law in a non-violent way.

---

**6. Conclusion**
Leaving a home marked by paternal violence is an act of symbolic separation that implies profound subjective reconfigurations. The distance from the father opens up possibilities for the reconstruction of desire, but it also brings challenges linked to trauma and the relationship with the law. Psychoanalysis offers tools to face these issues, helping the subject to reinscribe their history and find new forms of social ties.

Who starts the clinic?
The information- disinformation on social networks
Is there ethical management that can share all approaches?
Other models of knowledge and exercise, coaching, Angelo therapy and more... Opiniones de ello
Ethics in relation to the suffering of others

# ### How do you start the clinic?

The psychoanalytic clinic, within the framework of Freud and Lacan's training, is carried out by those who have undergone rigorous training, both theoretical and practical, which implies personal analysis and work on the analyst's desire. This ethical position is distinct from other therapeutic approaches, as it does not seek to direct or advise, but rather maintains a listening space that allows the subject to speak from their uniqueness.

However, nowadays, the term "clinic" has been expanded and used in contexts that range from coaching to alternative therapies such as angel therapy, often diluting the ethical rigor that underlies its practice in psychoanalysis.

---

**Information and disinformation on social networks**
In the contemporary panorama, social networks are a space where both valuable information and misinformation about mental health circulates. This can have different effects:

- **Democratized access:** The reach of certain discourses on the well-being has expanded, giving visibility to previously taboo topics.

- **Simplification risks:** Complex concepts (such as "trauma", "narcissism" or "depression") are presented in a reductionist or misinterpreted way, distorting their depth.

- **Massive influence:** Public figures or influencers without adequate training can popularize harmful ideas or based on pseudoscience.

---

**Is shared ethical management possible between approaches?**
Common ethical management would require:

1. **Recognizing differences:** Understand that not all approaches sharethe same epistemological foundations.

2. **Respect for the singularity of the subject:** Avoid uniformizing or normative practices that seek to adapt the subject to general standards.

3. **Transparency in the limits:** Each approach should explain its scope and what it can or cannot offer, without promising magical solutions.

However, the diversity of perspectives (psychoanalysis, coaching, alternative therapies, etc.) often clashes with the possibility of building a shared ethical consensus, since not all of them prioritize the suffering of the subject from a non-directive position.

---

**Other models of knowledge and its exercise**

1. **Coaching:**

- Emphasizes concrete objectives and action strategies.

- Criticism from psychoanalysis: It can standardize the subject under logics of productivity and personal success.

2. **Angelotherapy and alternative approaches:**

- Based on spiritual beliefs, they seek to heal through rituals or metaphysical connections.

- Criticism: The lack of scientific basis and the emotional dependence they generate in some cases can be problematic.

3. **Brief or cognitive-behavioral psychotherapies:**

- They work with delimited objectives and specific techniques to modify behaviors or thoughts.

- Ethical difference: They focus more on the adaptation of the subject than on their subjectivity.

---

**Ethics versus the suffering of the Other**
The suffering of the Other challenges the professional from the ethical position they adopt:

1. **Psychoanalysis:**

- Listen without imposing meanings, allow the subject to produce their own truth.

- Do not seek to normalize or offer immediate comfort, but work with the uniqueness of suffering.

2. **Other practices:**

- In some approaches, suffering is addressed from more directive logics, seeking quick solutions or normative redirections.

3. **Ethical risks in all approaches:**

- Turning the sufferer into an object of consumption, as occurs in social networks or spaces where suffering becomes a spectacle.

- Promising magical cures or baseless solutions.

---

**Conclusion**
The clinic, from psychoanalysis, requires an ethical commitment that prioritizes the singularity of the subject and respects his suffering without reducing it to generic categories. In a world where discourses on mental health proliferate, it is crucial to distinguish between information, misinformation and the interests behind each model of knowledge. Ethics in the face of the suffering of the Other must be the central axis, avoiding falling into practices that objectify or instrumentalize the subject.

Analysis of this on the Klein Bottle

# Analysis from the perspective of the **Klein Bottle** The **Klein Bottle** is a topological figure...

Analysis from the perspective of the **Klein Bottle** The **Klein Bottle** is a topological figure that lacks a clear distinction between interior and exterior, which makes it a useful conceptual model to think about the relationship between the subject, the Other and the discourses that circulate in our time, especially in the field of the clinic and ethics in the face of suffering.1. **The clinic as a continuous surface**

- In the **Klein Bottle**, the interior is not distinguished from the exterior: what seems "outside" is actually connected to what is "inside".

- In the clinic, this topology reflects how the subject's suffering is not something separate from social discourses, but rather these discourses (such as those spread on social networks) traverse the subject, shaping its symptoms and modes of enjoyment.

- Information and misinformation on social media act as turning points: the subject internalizes what seems external (concepts, popular diagnoses) and returns it to the world in new forms (demands for treatment, search for answers).

---

2. **The Other as the edge of the bottle**

- In Klein's bottle, the edge does not separate, but unites.

- From this perspective, the **Other of the clinic** (the edge of the bottle) is a kind of inflection point.:The analyst's figure, social networks, alternative approaches) is implicated in the subject's suffering. Ethics in the face of suffering cannot ignore this topological connection:
- The analyst cannot be placed as "outside" the subject's field of enjoyment.

- Disinformation on networks, although problematic, is also part of the subject's symbolic field, functioning as a new **edge** to structure his suffering.

---

3. **Different models of knowledge: intersections and exclusions**

- In Klein's bottle, there is no "pure" place separated from the twists: all discourses interpenetrate.

- **Psychoanalysis vs. other approaches:**
- Psychoanalysis maintains the subject's opacity in the face of his own desire, while approaches such as coaching seek clarity and immediate solutions.

- However, both discourses affect each other in their contact, since the contemporary subject can go from one approach to another according to his demands.

- This relationship could be understood as a continuous twist in the bottle, where the subject moves between different edges of knowledge without abandoning the global field of language and enjoyment.

---

4. **Ethics versus the suffering of the Other**

- In the topology of Klein's bottle, ethics cannot be thought of as a separation between the analyst and the sufferer, but as a twist:
- The analyst operates as a turning point that redirects the subject's demand, without trying to "fix" his suffering or reinforce his fantasies of completeness.

- In other approaches, such as angel therapy or coaching, the "Other" is presented as a provider of answers or solutions, but this position also introduces tensions, since the subject's enjoyment is reduced to consumption.

---

5. **Conclusion: the clinic as an open surface**
   Klein's bottle allows us to conceptualize the clinic as a space without fixed limits, where the subject's suffering, social discourses and clinical responses are continuously intertwined.

- **Psychoanalysis:** Maintains the structural void in the subject and enjoyment as something irreducible.

- **Other approaches:** They try to close the twist of the bottle by offering immediate solutions or certainties, but in the process they participate in the subject's field of enjoyment, forming part of the same topological circuit.

Thus, contemporary clinics must place themselves on the ethical edge of this bottle, recognizing that there is no "outside" of enjoyment or of the Other in the relationship with the subject's suffering.

Foreign language as a way of healing trauma, consistency

# ### Foreign Language as a Way of Healing Trauma: Consistency and Transformation ...

### Foreign Language as a Way of Healing Trauma: Consistency and Transformation

1. **Foreign Language and Trauma**

- Trauma often overwhelms the symbolic register, leaving the subject caught in a repetitive loop of unprocessed affects and fragmented representations.

- Learning or engaging in a foreign language introduces a **new symbolic order**, providing distance from the traumatic event by allowing the subject to reconfigure their relationship with language and meaning.

2. **Consistency in Foreign Language**

- **Foreign language creates a new consistency** for the subject by establishing an alternate framework of rules, grammar, and syntax. This structure offers stability and a sense of control, counteracting the fragmentation caused by trauma.

- The process of translation (literal and metaphorical) helps the subject articulate the unspeakable, offering a new medium for expression.

3. **Healing through the Symbolic Order**

- By inhabiting a foreign language, the subject reengages with the **symbolic network**, rediscovering pathways for meaning-making.

- This reengagement can be particularly effective because:
  - **The foreignness of the language:** Creates a buffer between the subject and the traumatic content.

  - **The detachment from the mother tongue:** Allows the subjectto experiment with different forms of articulation without the weight of past associations.

4. **New Subjective Position**

- The foreign language becomes a site of transformation:
  - It allows for the **reinvention of the self** , as the subject crafts a new identity within the contours of a new linguistic framework.

  - The gap between the mother tongue and the foreign language mirrors the subject’s **internal divisions** , offering a way to symbolize previously unarticulated aspects of their trauma.

5. **Clinical Implications**

- For clinicians, encouraging the exploration of foreign languages can serve as a tool for rebuilding the symbolic consistency of the subject.

- Incorporating foreign languages in therapy might include:
  - Translation exercises.

  - Writing or speaking in a foreign language to explore alternative modes of articulation.

  - Analyzing how the subject relates differently to their trauma when engaging in the foreign language.

6. **Conclusion** Foreign language functions as a **symbolic refuge** , offering the subject a space to process trauma and reestablish consistency. By navigating between two linguistic systems, the subject bridges gaps in their psyche, healing through the act of constructing meaning anew.

Principais conceitos Lacanianos sobre a Psicose

# ### Principais Conceitos Lacanianos sobre a Psicose ...

### Principais Conceitos Lacanianos sobre a Psicose

A psicose, no pensamento lacaniano, é uma estrutura clínica específica, distinta da neurose e da perversão. Os principais conceitos que embasam a compreensão da psicose são:

1. **Foraclusão do Nome-do-Pai**

- **Foraclusão** (do francês _forclusion_) refere-se à exclusão radical de um significante fundamental, o Nome-do-Pai, do campo do simbólico.

- Sem este significante, o sujeito não conta com a inscrição simbólica que organiza o desejo e sustenta a lei, resultando na ausência de um enquadramento para a realidade.

- A foraclusão é a causa da psicose, sendo distinta do recalque (neurose) ou da desmentida (perversão).

2. **Reintrodução do Real**

- Na psicose, o **real** irrompe na experiência do sujeito de forma intrusiva, uma vez que o simbólico não consegue mediar sua relação com a realidade.

- Exemplos incluem alucinações, delírios ou fenômenos corporais intrusivos.

3. **Holofrase**

- A holofrase é uma característica linguística frequentemente associada à psicose, onde significante e significado se unem de forma rígida, eliminando a polissemia e a possibilidade de metáfora.

- Isso compromete a estrutura simbólica e dificulta a circulação da linguagem.

4. **Clivagem entre Simbólico, Imaginário e Real (RSI)**

- Na psicose, as instâncias do **Real** , **Simbólico** e **Imaginário** não estão suficientemente ancoradas.

- A instabilidade dos registros leva a dificuldades na constituição da identidade, do corpo e das relações com o Outro.

5. **Empuxo-à-Mulher**

- Um fenômeno frequentemente observado em sujeitos psicóticos, o empuxo-à-mulher (_pousse-à-la-femme_) refere-se a um esforço para estabilizar a estrutura através da identificação com um significante feminino ou com o feminino enquanto sinthoma.

6. **O Delírio como Tentativa de Reconstrução**

- O delírio não é apenas uma ruptura, mas também uma tentativa do sujeito de dar sentido à desordem simbólica e estabilizar a relação com o Outro.

- É uma resposta criativa à fragmentação psíquica.

7. **A Relação com o Outro**

- O psicótico não encontra o **Outro simbólico** como referência de desejo ou lei, mas sim um Outro invasivo e persecutório.

- Isso explica experiências como paranoia e alucinações auditivas, onde o Outro aparece como absoluto.

8. **O Nome-do-Pai Metonímico**

- Em algumas formas de psicose, um significante substituto (ou compensatório) pode surgir para estabilizar parcialmente a estrutura.

- Este elemento pode ser encontrado em figuras como a religião, o amor, a criação artística, ou outras atividades que assumem a função de uma “amarração”.

9. **Schizo and Paranoia**

- Lacan does not adopt traditional psychiatric categories such as “schizophrenia” and “paranoia” in a rigid way, but proposes that both can be understood based on the particularities of foreclosure and the relationship with the Other.

10. **The Body in Psychosis**

- The relationship of the psychotic subject with the body is marked by disintegration and strangeness. Phenomena such as mutilation, obsession with the body or a feeling of intrusion are common.

### Clinical Implications

- In the clinic, the objective is not to cure psychosis, but to seek **symbolic ties** that allow the subject to stabilize his relationship with the Other and the world.

- Listening should consider the subject's attempts to create unique solutions for his structure, such as delirium, art or other singular practices.

Mental automatism, open unconscious, communication scheme

# ### Mental Automatism, Open Unconscious and the Communication Scheme ...

### Mental Automatism, Open Unconscious and the Communication Scheme

1. **Mental Automatism**

- Concept derived from classical psychiatry (Clérambault), introduced by Lacan in Psychoanalysis to describe the psychotic experience in which thoughts, words or images seem to be produced in an automatic and alienated manner.

- The subject perceives these manifestations as external or intrusive, which often characterizes auditory or visual hallucinations.

- **Clinical example**: hearing voices commenting on the subject's actions, as if they were an external observer.

##### Lacan:

- Mental automatism is understood as a break in the signifying chain.

- It reveals the **unconscious in the open**, that is, the unconscious that is not repressed, but exposed directly to the subject.

---

2. **Unconscious in the Open**

- In psychosis, the foreclosure of the Name-of-the-Father prevents the symbolic from functioning as a mediator of desire and reality.

- What is repressed in neurosis (according to Freud) appears directly in psychosis, without the filter of the symbolic, causing strangeness and anguish.

- The psychotic subject does not interpret what arises, but experiences it in a raw way, as something external.

##### Characteristics:

- The unconscious arises without symbolic mediation or censorship.

- Direct manifestations include hallucinations, voices, delusional ideas, or "thoughts that are not mine".

##### Relationship with the Real:

- For Lacan, this demonstrates the intrusion of the **real** into psychic experience, without the symbolic structure to contain it.

---

3. **Communication Scheme (Lacan, 1955)** Lacan's communication scheme organizes the interaction between the **Symbolic**, **Imaginary** and **Real** registers, explaining the rupture in psychosis.

- **Addressing the Other**:

In neurosis, speech is addressed to the **symbolic Other** (place of language, law, interpretation).

In psychosis, the symbolic Other is not constituted, resulting in a direct relationship with the **real Other**, which is intrusive and persecutory.

- **Elements of the scheme**:

- The **subject** (S): the one who speaks or thinks.

- The **Other** (A): the recipient or symbolic place of language.

- The **message**: what is said.

- The **code**: shared rules that organize communication (absent or fragmented in psychosis).

##### In Psychosis:

- The code is replaced by the **holophrase** (a rigid fusion between signifier and signified).

- The message does not circulate, and the subject experiences the Other as oppressive or invasive.

---

4. **Clinical Implications**

- Mental automatism and the unconscious in the open show the absence of symbolic mediation.

- The analyst does not seek repression (as in neurosis), but rather to enable the construction of a **stabilizing symptom** or a binding that contains the intrusive real.

- Listening in psychosis considers the subject as the producer of an **attempt to make sense** in the face of what escapes him.

---

These concepts show how the structure of psychosis is marked by the rupture in communication and the intrusion of the reall, providing a basis for clinical practice in the psychoanalytic approach.

List of bodily phenomena with President Schreber

# The bodily phenomena experienced by Daniel Paul Schreber, described in his \*\*"Memoirs of a Nervous ...

The bodily phenomena experienced by Daniel Paul Schreber, described in his **"Memoirs of a Nervous Patient"** and analyzed by Freud and Lacan, include a wide range of psychosomatic manifestations and delusional experiences. The following is a detailed list of these bodily phenomena:

---

**1. Bodily transformations**

- Sensation that his body was being **transformed into a female body** , as part of his fantasy of being impregnated by God.

- **Feeling of feminization** : perception of changes in his genitals and other parts of the body, related to the "pull towards woman".

---

**2. Intrusive bodily perceptions**

- **Tactile hallucinations** : sensations of being touched or manipulated by forces external.

- **Nerve currents**: Schreber believed that "divine rays" passed through his body, causing pleasure or pain.

---

**3. Body dissociation** - He felt as if parts of his body were no longer his or were under the control of another entity (the "Other").

- Experience that his body was **divided or disintegrated**.

---

**4. Excessive body eroticism** - He felt exaggerated pleasure or pain in specific parts of his body, often interpreted as **divine manipulation**.

- Reports of orgasmic sensations related to his transformation into a woman.

---

**5. Eating and physiological disorders** - He perceived the process of eating and digestion as something governed by external forces.

- Sensation of **external control over his basic bodily functions** (urination, bowel movements, eating).

---

**6. Somatic hallucinations**

- Schreber reported **unexplained pains**, pressures and sensations in various parts of his body, often attributed to the actions of "divine rays".

- Experience of **internal dismemberment**, as if his organs were being removed or replaced.

---

**7. Experience of bodily immortality**

- He believed that his body was preparing to become immortal, part of its function as a mediator between God and the world.

---

**8. Voices that invaded the body**

- Schreber believed that God and other entities spoke to him directly through his organs, especially his genitals.

- He believed that these voices made him feel pain or pleasure in specific parts of his body.

---

**9. Relationship with bodily space**

- He perceived his body as a battlefield between divine and demonic forces.

- He felt that parts of his body were **expanding or retracting**, as if they had no defined limits.

---

**10. Identification of the body with the universe**

- Schreber described a symbolic identification between his body and the universe, where his bodily transformations were part of a cosmic plan.

----**Psychoanalytic Readings**

- **Freud**: He related these phenomena to the **repression of homosexual desire** and the return of the repressed in delirium.

- **Lacan**: He emphasized the failure of the **foraclusion of the Name-of-the-Father**, which led Schreber to a direct relationship with the **real of the body**, without symbolic mediation.

- **Body and language**: The bodily phenomena in Schreber reflect the psychotic's attempt to organize his body in relation to the symbolic, often without success.

These bodily phenomena, marked by the intrusion of reality, offer a window to understanding the psychotic structure and its clinical challenges.

List of phenomena in autism

# In autism, the phenomena presented are quite varied and reflect challenges in the areas of social interaction, communication, behavior, and sensory processes. The following is a detailed list of these phenomena:

---

**1. Communication and Languageagem**

- **Mutism**: total absence of speech.

- **Echolalia**: repetition of words or phrases said by other people.

- **Literal use of language**: difficulty understanding metaphors, sarcasm, or humor.

- **Long monologues**: talking about specific interests without considering the interlocutor.

---

**2. Social Interaction**

- **Difficulty establishing eye contact** or maintaining visual interaction.

- **Social withdrawal**: preference for solitary activities.

- **Lack of response to social cues**: difficulty recognizing facial expressions, tone of voice, or body language.

- **Atypical empathy**: difficulty identifying or responding to the feelings of others, or emotional hypersensitivity.

---

**3. Sensory Processing**

- **Hypersensitivity**: intense responses to lights, sounds, textures, smells, or tastes.

- **Hyposensitivity**: lack of reaction to sensory stimuli (e.g., pain or heat).

- **Self-stimulation (stimming)**: repetitive behaviors such as rocking the body, flapping hands, spinning objects, among others.

- **Fixed interest in specific sensory stimuli**: fascination with repetitive lights, patterns, or sounds.

---

**4. Repetitive and Restrictive Behaviors** - **Rigid routines**: great discomfort with changes or interruptions in routines.

- **Intense and restricted interests**: deep focus on a specific, sometimes unusual, topic.

- **Repetitive movements**: spinning, tapping, lining up objects, walking in circles.

- **Resistance to novelty**: reluctance to try new foods, clothes, or environments.

---

**5. Cognitive** - **Exceptional memory**: ability to remember specific details, sometimes in an impressive way.

- **Detailed thinking**: focus on specific parts rather than the whole.

- **Difficulty with abstraction**: prefer concrete and visual information.

- **Savant skills**: in some cases, exceptional skills in music, mathematics, art, among others.

---

**6. Relational Behaviors**: **Unusual attachment to objects**: preference for specific toys or unconventional objects.

- **Difficulty initiating or maintaining friendships**.

- **Rule-guided interaction**: structured social approach, based on patterns or scripts.

---

**7. Bodily and Somatic**: **Atypical muscle tone**: hypo- or hypertonia.

- **Different gait or posture**: walking on tiptoe or specific body positions.

- **Self-harm**: hitting, biting, or scratching oneself as a way of dealing with frustrations.

- **Eating or sensory disorders with food**: restricted food preferences due to textures, colors, or odors.

---

**8. Relationship with Others and the World**

- **Difficulty understanding social roles**: interpreting or playing the role of an authority figure, friend, etc.

- **Lack of social reciprocity**: difficulty sharing interests or emotions.

- **Interest in details of the environment**: observing patterns on the floor, lining up objects, or collecting specific items.

---

**9. Emotional aspects**

- **Intense anxiety**: especially in new or unpredictable situations.

- **Emotional outbursts**: difficulty regulating emotions.

- **Atypical emotional expressions**: unexpected laughter or crying, seemingly out of context.

---

**10. Body Phenomena Related to Language**

- **Absence of metaphor** : difficulty in connecting words to multiple or symbolic meanings.

- **Rejection or resistance to the symbolic** : preference for literal and concrete information.

---

**Psychoanalytic Reading**

- **Foraclusion** : gaps in the inscription of the symbolic that lead the subject to organize his experience in the real of the body and in the imaginary.

- **Resistance to the Other** : search for autonomy in the face of the demands of the Other, sometimes seen as refusal.

- **Letter on the body** : stabilization through fixed forms ofand enjoyment linked to objects or repetitions.

These phenomena reflect the singularity of the autistic experience and offer multiple entry points for the psychoanalytic clinic.

Function of horror in film and TV as a form of sublimation

# The **function of horror in film and TV** can be understood as a form of psychoanalytic sublimation, with implications for both the spectator and the culture in general. Below are some analyses of this function:

---

**1. Horror as Sublimation**

- **Confrontation with the Real** :
  Horror exposes spectators to the **Lacanian Real** – that which is outside the symbolic and which causes anguish because it is impossible to name or symbolize. Monsters, ghosts and inexplicable threats represent figures of the Real, transformed into imaginary narratives that can be endured.

- **Transformation of Enjoyment**:
  The anguish and fear awakened in horror are converted into **aesthetic enjoyment**, allowing the subject to experience the unbearable in a controlled manner.

- **The Letter in Horror**:
  Horror writes the experience of limits on the viewer's body, such as the scream or the emotional discharge that sublimates internal violence in a safe way.

---

**2. Horror and Desire** - **Exposure of Forbidden Desire**:
Horror reveals repressed or socially unacceptable desires in a symbolic way. Themes such as cannibalism, death, betrayal or sexual violence appear in distorted but symbolically charged forms.

---**Fantasies of Transgression**:
Horror films and series allow the viewer to explore fantasies of transgression – facing monsters, surviving apocalypses, defying taboos – without the real consequences of acting on these desires.

---

**3. Horror and the Clinical Structure**

- **Neurosis** :
  For neurotic subjects, horror allows them to confront internal fears (phobias, anxieties) in a projected way, offering a safe space to deal with psychic conflicts.

- **Psychosis** :
  Horror can mirror the functioning of psychotic subjects, where the Real invades everyday experience, and the symbolic fails to organize the experience. However, structured horror films can help stabilize, providing narratives for what is perceived as chaos.

- **Perversion** :
  In perversion, horror can offer an arena for enjoyment related to the confrontation with the limits of the Law, exploring themes such as submission, torture or control.

---

**4. Horror as Social Criticism**

- **Figure of the Monster as Metaphor** :
  Monsters represent the anxieties and tensions of society – inequalities, wars, climate change, identity crises.

- **Symbolic Violence** :
  Horror often dramatizes structural and cultural violence that is unrecognized or repressed, making it visible and debatable.

- **Denunciation and Catharsis** :
  Through violence and the grotesque, horror allows the audience to experience a form of **emotional purging** (catharsis), while also reflecting on injustices or universal issues.

---

**5. Relationship with the Other**
---**Solidarity and Community** :
Horror narratives often deal with the subject's relationship with the group – how to survive together, face the unknown Other or deal with internal betrayal.

---**The Other as Monster** :
Horror works with the idea that the **Other** (the strange, the different) is both a threat and a fascination, putting the viewer in contact with the foreigner within themselves.

----**6. Ethical and Aesthetic Function**

- **Education for the Incomprehensible** :
  Horror teaches us to deal with what we do not understand – death, the afterlife, violence – in an imaginative way.

- **Aesthetics of the Sublime** :
  Horror allows the viewer to access the sublime, that which is grandiose and terrifying, expanding the limits of the imagination.

---

In short, horror works as a psychological tooliquitous and cultural means of dealing with the Real, channeling aggressive or death-related impulses, exploring repressed fantasies and reflecting on society's challenges and fears. It is a form of sublimation that translates the unnamable into narratives that can be consumed, discussed and understood.

Horror in the family: crimes of passion

# **Crimes of passion** in the family context can be analyzed as extreme manifestations of psychic, social and cultural conflicts that involve desire, enjoyment and the impasses of the social bond. In the field of Psychoanalysis, these occurrences often refer to the unconscious dynamics of the subject in relation to the Other.**1. Horror in the Family Context**

- **The Family as a Place of Enjoyment**:
  The family is the space where the most intimate and intense relationships unfold, and it is also the privileged place for confrontation with desire and castration.
- The **father**, the **mother**, the **spouse** or the **children** can be invested as figures of the Other who regulate or prohibit enjoyment.

- When symbolic norms fail, excessive enjoyment can lead to drastic ruptures.

- **The Horror of Everyday Life**:
  Crimes of passion often reveal the latent horror in the family's daily life, exposing death drives, jealousy, resentments and repressions that remain hidden under the appearance of stability.

---

**2. The Crime of Passion as an Act**

- **The Act as a Response to the Real**:
  In the case of crimes of passion, the violent act can be a desperate attempt to suture a wound in the field of the **Real** – the unbearable feeling of betrayal, abandonment or rejection. - It is a way for the subject to try to reestablish control in the face of a symbolic loss (love, recognition, social position).

- **Fantasy and Crime**:
  Crime of passion is usually preceded by obsessive fantasies in which the subject tries to deal with the anguish caused by the Other, be it a partner or a rival. Fantasy structures the crime as an imaginary solution to the conflict.

---

**3. Psychic Dynamics in Crime of Passion**

- **Neurosis**:
  In neurosis, crime of passion may arise as an extreme response to the **feeling of castration** and the impossibility of fulfilling the desire. Pathological jealousy often occupies a central place.
- Example: The subject believes that eliminating the Other will restore "order" to his/her life.

- **Psychosis**:
  In psychosis, crime may be related to the **disorganization of the symbolic bond** and the invasion of the Real. The loss of connection with reality can lead to extreme acts that seem "justified" in the subject's delusional logic.
- Example: A psychotic subject may perceive the spouse as a conspiring enemy and act to "protect himself".

- **Perversion**:
  In perversion, the crime of passion can be a way of defying the symbolic Law, affirming enjoyment directly against the prohibition.
- Example: The perverted subject may interpret the crime as a "right" or as a way of completely controlling the Other.

---

**4. Horror in Social Discourse**

- **Pathologization and Romanticization**:
- In society, crimes of passion are often romanticized as acts of extreme love, which masks the dynamics of violence and control.

- At the same time, they are pathologized, being attributed to "sick" individuals, without considering cultural and unconscious factors.

- **The Function of Horror**:
  These crimes evoke horror because they expose the fragility of human relationships and the destructive potential that emerges when the subject is confronted with **lack**.

---

**5. The Family as a Scene of Horror**

- **Intergenerational Trauma**:
  Crimes of passion are often perpetuated in contexts where symbolic or real violence is transmitted between generations.
- Example: The repetition of patterns of abuse, abandonment or exacerbated jealousy.

- **The Mother, the Father and the Child in Horror**:
- The figure of the **mother** canbe associated with excessive enjoyment, incessant demands or rejection.

- The figure of the **father**, instead of interdicting, can become violent or absent, destabilizing the family balance.

- The child is often the **witness of the horror**, being directly or indirectly involved in the violent acts.

- ... - **The Ethics of Psychoanalysis**:
  In the face of the horror of crimes of passion, Psychoanalysis invites us to **endure the lack**, instead of trying to fill it with extreme acts.

---

**Conclusion**
The horror of crimes of passion within the family is a testament to what happens when enjoyment becomes unregulated, when castration fails and the symbolic bond is broken. Psychoanalysis offers tools to understand and elaborate on these acts, seeking to subvert the cycle of violence and enable other forms of subjectivation and social bond.

Subjective position, mode of enjoyment: no one offered me anything, it is an insultThis content may violate our [usage policies](https://openai.com/policies/usage-policies) .

# This subjective position, expressed in the sentence "Nobody offered me anything, it's an insult", can be analyzed psychoanalytically in relation to the subject's **desire**, **demand** and modes of **enjoyment**.

---

**1. Subjective Position**

- **Wounded narcissism**:
  This stance can arise from a feeling of non-recognition by the Other. The subject interprets the lack of an offer as a direct affront to his existence or value.
- The "nothing offered" is read as a **refusal** or indifference on the part of the Other, which destabilizes the narcissism and reveals the **structural dependence** of desire in relation to the Other.

- **Demand and resentment**:
  The subject, when expecting something from the Other (attention, care, material goods), places himself in the position of the one who demands. When this demand is not met, resentment arises, which is ultimately a return of unsatisfied desire.

---

**2. Modalities of Enjoyment**
---**Enjoyment of resentment**:
In the absence of an offer from the Other, the subject can enjoy his own resentment, making the "insult" a way of situating himself in the social bond. This enjoyment keeps the subject trapped in the complaint and in the position of victim.

---**Phallic enjoyment**:
Phallic enjoyment is related to the logic of "having". Not being offered "nothing" directly touches on the dimension of "having something" as a guarantee of value. The absence of the offer is experienced as an unbearable castration.

---**Deadly enjoyment**:
When resentment is taken to the extreme, there may be a refusal to sustain the social bond, leading the subject to a destructive position with himself and with others.

---**3. The Other and the Insolence**

- **The Other as provider** :
  This position implies that the subject expects something from the Other – be it recognition, support or material goods. When the Other does not respond, the subject perceives this as an “insolence”, that is, a transgression against his/her dignity.

- **The undoing of the illusion** :
  In Psychoanalysis, the “nothing” offered by the Other can be read as a confrontation with the **structural lack** that inhabits both the subject and the Other. This “insolence” is not something imposed, but the revelation that the Other does not have what is expected.

---

**4. Ethical Position and Possibilities of Subversion**

- **Assuming the lack** :
  Psychoanalysis proposes that the subject, instead of insisting on the unsatisfied demand, can recognize and appropriate his/her own lack, subverting the logic of thessentiment and deadly enjoyment.

- **Displacing the complaint**:
  Subverting the position of "victim of the Other" can open space for new forms of social bonding, where lack is endured as constitutive and not as an insult.

- **Creating something from nothing**:
  Instead of remaining fixated on the expectation that the Other will offer something, the subject can find creative ways of producing meaning and enjoyment from what he or she lacks.

---

**Conclusion**
The phrase "Nobody offered me anything, it's an insult" reveals a subjective position linked to dependence on the Other to sustain desire. This position, while denouncing dissatisfaction, also shows the possibility of subversion: recognizing lack as structural and transforming it into a driving force for creation and autonomy. The challenge, in Psychoanalysis, is to move away from the logic of resentment and demand and enter into the ethics of enduring lack.

Ignorance in Lacan

# **Ignorance** in Lacan is not merely the absence of knowledge, but an active and structured position ...

**Ignorance** in Lacan is not merely the absence of knowledge, but an active and structured position within the subject's relationship with knowledge and with the Other. It is a concept that is linked to desire, enjoyment, and the function of the unconscious.

---

**1. Ignorance as a refusal of knowledge**

- **Active refusal**:
  Ignorance can be understood as a defense against knowledge. Knowledge, especially in the context of Psychoanalysis, is always something that implies a confrontation with the truth of desire and lack. Ignorance functions as a **repression**: the subject avoids knowing that which would disturb his subjective position.

- **Relationship with the unconscious**:
  Lacan states that "the unconscious is structured like a language". Ignorance can be seen as a **structured lack of knowledge** of this language, a resistance to the recognition of the unconscious and its effects.

---

**2. Ignorance and enjoyment**

- **Defense against enjoyment** :
  Knowledge is always related to enjoyment, as it implies a proximity to the object a. Ignorance, in this sense, is a form of defense against the excess of enjoyment that knowledge can trigger.

- **Enjoyment of ignorance** :
  Paradoxically, the subject can find enjoyment in ignorance, by refusing to leave the position of not knowing. This enjoyment is particularly evident in situations in which ignorance serves to sustain a fantasy or avoid anxiety.

---

**3. Ignorance and transference**

- **In the position of the analysand** :
  In the analytic setting, the analysand can resist knowledge through ignorance. This is not an obstacle, but rather part of the analytic work, as it reveals the structure of the subject's desire.

- **In the analyst's position**:
  Lacan emphasizes that the analyst occupies a position of "not knowing". This "not knowing" is not ignorance in the common sense, but an **ethical position** that allows the analyst to sustain the emptiness necessary for the analysand's desire to unfold.

---

**4. Ignorance and the Other**

- **Ignorance as a relationship with the knowledge of the Other**:
  For Lacan, knowledge is located in the Other. Ignorance can be a strategy of the subject to avoid confrontation with the knowledge that the Other supposedly holds.

- **Ignorance and submission**:
  Ignorance can also be a form of submission to the knowledge of the Other, a way of avoiding subjective responsibility, delegating the function of knowing to the Other.

---

**5. Ethical implications of ignorance**

- **Ignorance and truth**:
  Ignorance, in psychoanalysis, is always in relation to truth. It can be a refusal of the truth, but also a strategy to deal with the unbearable that truth represents.

- **Knowledge and desire**:
  Lacan teaches us that knowledge is never complete; it is always lacking. Ignorance can, paradoxically, open space for desire, because it is in the lack of knowledge that desire is articulated.

---

**Conclusion**

Ignorance, in Lacan, is not a deficiency, but a subjective position that reveals the way the subject deals withwith knowledge, desire and enjoyment. It can be a defense against knowledge, but also a way of accessing truth and the unconscious. In the clinic, ignorance is not an obstacle to be overcome, but a phenomenon to be interpreted and worked on in transference.

Ignorance in the RSI

# Ignorance in Lacan's **RSI** (Real, Symbolic and Imaginary) scheme can be analyzed as a subjective position that operates in each of the registers, marking different forms of defense and the subject's relationship with knowledge and desire.

---

**1. Ignorance in the Imaginary**
In the register of the Imaginary, ignorance is linked to the subject's specular relationship with the other. It manifests itself in:

- **Rejection of otherness**: The subject, trapped in imaginary identification, can ignore difference and lack, preferring an illusion of completeness.

- **Narcissism**: Ignoring the knowledge of the Other to maintain one's own idealized image or protect oneself from symbolic castration.

- **Imaginary conflicts**: Ignorance in the Imaginary can lead to rivalry and aggression, since the knowledge of the Other is perceived as a threat to the subject's identity.

---

**2. Ignorance in the Symbolic** In the register of the Symbolic, ignorance is related to the refusal of knowledge as language and the denial of the function of lack.

- **Rejection of castration**: Ignoring the symbolic inscription of castration as a structuring limit of desire.

- **Defense against the unconscious**: Ignorance in the Symbolic manifests itself as resistance to unconscious knowledge, denying its structuring function and trying to avoid the anguish it causes.

- **Structured ignorance**: Here, ignorance is not simply the absence of knowledge, but a position that organizes the subject in relation to the Other, often sustaining fantasies and defensive positions.

---

**3. Ignorance in the Real**

In the register of the Real, ignorance is articulated with the impossible, with that which escapes symbolization and the imaginary. It can be seen as a defense against the encounter with the impossible to say.

- **Ignorance as a defense against enjoyment**: The subject may refuse to know about the enjoyment that structures him, because this knowledge would bring about confrontation with the excess and the unnamable of the Real.

- **Denial of trauma**: Ignorance in the Real operates as an attempt to avoid the impact of trauma, which is that which returns insistently, but which cannot be fully integrated.

- **Foreclosure**: In extreme cases, such as in psychosis, ignorance in the Real can take the form of a foreclosure, in which a central signifier is rejected from the Symbolic and returns in the Real.

---

**4. Articulation between RSI and ignorance**

- Ignorance can be understood as a form of defense that runs through the three registers. It operates:

- In the **Imaginary**, as a rejection of difference and attachment to the illusion of completeness.

- In the **Symbolic**, as resistance to the unconscious and castration.

- In the **Real**, as a refusal to encounter the impossible and excess enjoyment.

- **Symptom and ignorance**: The resolution of the tensions between RSI involves the elaboration of a symptom that articulates these registers. Ignorance can be worked on in the analysis as a way of constructing knowledge about one's own desire and enjoyment.

---

**Conclusion**
Ignorance in the RSI scheme reveals the different ways in which the subject deals with knowledge, desire and enjoyment in each register. It is not just an absence of knowledge, but a structured subjective position that protects the subject from distressing confrontations with castration, the unconscious and the impossible. Psychoanalysis, when interpreting these manifestations, seeks to shift this position and pave the way for a knowledge that articulates desire and supports the subject in the face of its lack.

Punitive superego, sadomasochistic fantasies, being beaten by the father, self-sabotage, self-destruction, analysis under the Klein Bottle

# The analysis of thesequestions from the perspective of the **Klein Bottle** can offer a topological view...

The analysis of these questions from the perspective of the **Klein Bottle** can offer a topological view that articulates the subjective dynamics of the **punitive superego**, **sadomasochistic fantasies**, **self-sabotage** and **self-destruction** in relation to the RSI (Real, Symbolic and Imaginary) registers. The Klein Bottle, as a non-orientable surface, is a metaphor for the structure of the subject and for the continuous movements between inside-outside, enjoyment-lack, punishment-desire.

---

**1. Punitive superego: the voice of the Other that never ceases**
The superego, as a punitive instance, can be understood as a relentless voice of the Other that insists, imposing impossible mandates of satisfaction.

- **Klein Bottle** :
- The dynamics of the superego are reflected in the infinite and cyclical character of the Klein Bottle. Punishment doubles in on itself, not allowing a clear way out. The subject, in trying to escape the demands of the superego, reconnects with the enjoyment that returns in the same movement.

- The impossibility of separating "inside" (guilty subject) from "outside" (mandate of the Other) represents the fusion between the desire of the Other and the enjoyment of the subject.

---

**2. Sadomasochistic fantasies: enjoyment in punishment**
Sadomasochistic fantasies articulate the relationship of the subject with enjoyment and with the Other, where the position of submission or dominance is crossed by desire and guilt.

- **Klein bottle**:
- Sadism and masochism appear as two sides of the same surface. The subject who is "beaten by the father" (masochistic position) can, at the same time, identify with the aggressor (sadistic position), without a clear delimitation between these positions.

- The Klein Bottle reflects this continuous inversion: the subject, in seeking punishment, enjoys both as a "victim" and as a "executioner", indicating that both positions feed off each other.

---

**3. Self-sabotage: return of enjoyment in failure**
Self-sabotage can be seen as an expression of enjoyment linked to the superego, where failure or inability to achieve reinforces the punishment that feeds the enjoyment circuit.

- **Klein Bottle** :
- Self-sabotage is like a continuous movement through the Klein Bottle, where the subject tries to achieve a goal, but in the process, returns to the starting point (failure).

- The "inside" (desire for success) mixes with the "outside" (superego's mandate of failure), resulting in a position where the subject is trapped in the enjoyment of repetition.

---

**4. Self-destruction: dissolution in absolute enjoyment**
Self-destruction occurs when the subject, unable to sustain desire, abandons the search for lack and launches himself directly into the Real of enjoyment, where annihilation appears as an impossible solution.

- **Klein Bottle** :
- The self-destructive movement can be read as a path along the surface of the Bottle, where there is no exteriority to escape enjoyment. The subject seeks to "leave" his condition, but always finds himself in the same place.

- Self-destruction, therefore, is the attempt to break with the continuity of the Bottle, something that results in the encounter with the Real of the impossible.

---

**5. "Being beaten by the father": the relationship with the enjoyment of the Other**
The subject who "is beaten by the father" articulates himself with the fantasy of submission to the enjoyment of the Other, often associated with guilt and the desire for reparation.

- **Klein Bottle**:
- The relationship with the father, as a figure of the superego and enjoyment, is a continuous inversion between the role of victim (the one who gets beaten) and accomplice of enjoyment (the one who supports the father's authority).
- The subject, by "getting beaten", perpetuates the Bottle circuit, where guilt and enjoyment feed off each other.

---

**Conclusion**
The Klein Bottle offers a way of thinking about subjective dynamics as a continuous and non-orientable circuit, where the punitive superego, sadomasochistic fantasies, self-sabotage and self-destruction become modes of relating to enjoyment and the Other.

- The challenge of the Klein BottleThe goal lies in making the subject recognize these dynamics and construct a **symptom** that allows the articulation of desire with lack, breaking the infinite repetition of deadly enjoyment.

- The topology of the Bottle also points to the impossibility of a definitive separation between inside and outside, enjoyment and lack, indicating that psychoanalysis works on the articulation of these borders to sustain the subject in the social bond.

Daughters of psychopathic mothers
A psychoanalyst listens to a crazy mother
Quote Psychoanalysts who are daughters of serious mental patients

# The issue of **daughters of psychopathic mothers** or mothers with serious clinical structures, such as psychosis...

The issue of **daughters of psychopathic mothers** or mothers with serious clinical structures, such as psychosis or perversion, brings to light a complex field in Psychoanalysis. The impact of the relationship with a “crazy” mother is central to understanding how the subject articulates his desire, his lack and his enjoyment. The analyst's **listening** in these cases needs to take into account the singularity of the maternal bond and its repercussions on the daughter's subjectivity.

---
